484 a—485 A We have now to
shew that Philosophers, as defined by us,
should be entrusted with the government.
It is they alone who, by virtue of the Ldeal
in their souls, are able to guard the laws
and institutions of @ city. We shall
therefore make them our Guardians, uf
they possess the necessary practical qual-

cations. A study of their nature will
shew that it is possible for them to unite
both kinds of requisites.

484 A 1 Sia pakpod — Adyovu:
“through the conclusion of a somewhat
lengthy argument.’ dvefeAPSv70s is in-
transitive, as Schneider saw: cf. Laws
805B aANd yap elroy Tov Mev Abyov édoat
Sic~eNOetv, eb SveAOdvros dé otrw 7d
doxodv alpetcOa Setv. (The reference in
elroy is to 799E Kav 5réE0d0s atrn bdH
cxo0ca TéXos ixavds dy pnvicee KT.)
Cf also Dem. ix Mid. 84. The word
SveEeAOdvTos is not otiose, because it is
not till the very end of the argument that
the guddcopos is discovered (v 480 4).
The mistaken notion (held by Stallbaum)
that the word must be transitive induced
Herwerden (Mnem. N. S. XIX p. 333) to
propose diefeAPodau, a conjecture repeated
also by Richards. Baiter (after Hermann
and Ast) reads dueteOdvres with three
inferior Mss, as if the philosophers had
“run the gauntlet of the argument through
which their nature is revealed” (J. and
C.). Tob. Ad-you (found in a few Mss) is
favoured by Stallbaum, and suggested as

A. P. IL

an alternative also by Herwerden, as if
dud paxpod Twds could mean ‘at some
length.’ The first hand in & omits dd,
but it occurs in all the other Mss. None
of these expedients is nearly so good as
the reading of the best Mss, if Schneider’s
explanation be adopted. paxpod has
also caused difficulty, since the in-
vestigation extends over only six pages
of Stephanus: see Krohn 2. St. pp.
103 ff. By Pfleiderer (Zur Loisung etc.
p. 54), who maintains (in partial agree-
ment with Spengel) that v 471 C—VII
(inclusive) embodies the dialogue ®:A6-
codos announced in the beginning of- the
Politicus and Sophist, waxpod is hailed as
a significant Japsus calami, and referred
to the investigations of the Sephist, Huthy-
demus and Politicus. But waxpod is quali-
fied by 716s, and surely 474C—480A may
be described as ‘a somewhat lengthy
enquiry.’ There is no allusion to the

totle speaks in Met. N 3. rogi* ” ff.

485A—487A The philosophic nature
loves eternal and changeless Being in ws
entirety. It follows that the philoso-
pher naturally loves Truth, despises the
pleasures of the body, ts temperate, free
from avarice, high-minded, courageous,
just and gentle. He is also quick to
learn, retentive in memory, not given
to extravagance in conduct, but modest and
well-bred. To such men, when years and
education have perfected their natural
qualities, we may fairly entrust our city.

485A ff. This section should be com-
pared on the one hand with II 375 A—
376C, 377 B—III 391 £, and on the other
with VII 535A, Bz. In Book II the
natural qualities insisted on were pri-
marily moral; here and in vii they are
primarily intellectual. This is in har-
mony with the difference between the
earlier and later schemes of education:
for the basis of the first was 6p0% 56a,
whereas that of the second is émorjun.

There is little or no indication to shew
that even the apyovres of I—IV knew or
aspired to the Ideas (see 497Cz.) and
the étxovpot certainly did not. Krohn
is, in a certain sense, right when he
maintains that in VI—VII we have “einen
neuen Archontenstand und eine neue Ar-
chontendisciplin” (7%. Sz. p. 107), but the
distinction of the ‘golden’ and ‘silver’
races in Ill 415 Aff. prepares us for a
more thorough-going discrimination be-
tween the two higher classes than was
attempted in the earlier sketch, and we
must of course remember that the new
discipline is not intended to supersede,
but to supervene upon the old. See also
Hirzel Der Dialog 1 p. 236.

4858 8 ékelyns THs Ovolas. For the
genitive cf. IV 445 E 2.

g yevérews Kal pOopds have not yet
been employed in this half-technical sense
(Krohn P/. St. p. 112). The substance
of the Ideas always ‘is’: that of pheno-
mena ‘is driven to and fro by generation
and destruction’—by generation when
it becomes determined in one particular
direction (e.g. Kaddv, trmos, vO pwrros),
by destruction when it loses that par-
ticular determination and puts om an-
other. Cf. Vv 479A, B. Plato’s form of
expression seems to imply that there is a
sort of ovcta or substratum in pheno-
mena. Ata later stage he seems to have
identified this with space—the éxuaryetov

16 ékévTas elvar is ‘voluntarily,’ not

wed80s should be understood in its
strict Platonic sense, as ‘ignorance in the
soul respecting the truth’ (II 382 B 7.).
The politician who knows not the Ideal
is, according to Plato, a liar, not the
statesman who employs for example
KAfpol twes Kouwol to attain his Ideal.
There is absolutely no reason to suppose
(with Bosanquet) that Plato means to
withdraw from the regulations of v 4604.

31 Xpypara—Samavys : ‘wealth with
its accompaniment of lavish outlay.’ pera
modnjs Samdvys should not, I think, be
taken with omovSdferar (Schneider, D.
and V., J-and C., although Jowett’s trans-
lation takes the correct view), but rather
with xpnuara. Herwerden formerly ex-
plained damdvqs as=Ths To) Samavay émt-
Ouulas, but afterwards (nem. N.S. XIX
p- 333) took it to mean ‘“‘pecunia cuius

vill 550 D and Laws 718 A. This view

necessary to suppose that damayn means
more than simply ‘outlay.’

ABGA 2 avedevdepias. dvehevdepla
or cpxpodoyla is in Plato the antithesis
of trepngpavia: cf. It 391 C and Crétias
112 c. The virtuous meah is peyado-
arpémeva, which is a sort of highminded-
ness (cf. 503 C): hence fueryadom pémreta,
just below and peyaromrperys in the
summary at 487 A. Plato does not, like
Aristotle (Zh. Wic. 1v cc. 4—6), restrict

pecuniary dealings, although ¢v\0xXpy-
parla, for example, is a symptom 0
dvedevdepia (II 391 C)-

rod $Aov Kal wavTos. Cf. Theat.
1738 ff. This and the following sentence
admirably describe the peculiar genius of

them with Goethe’s noble characterisation
of Plato: ‘Er dringt in die Tiefen, mehr
um sie mit seinem Wesen auszufiillen,
als um sie zu erforschen. Er bewegt
sich nach der Hdhe, mit Sehnsucht
seines Ursprungs wieder theilhaft zu wer-
den. Alles, was er aussert, bezieht sich
auf ein ewig Ganzes, Gutes, Wahres,
Schénes, dessen Forderung er in jedem
Busen aufzuregen strebt.”

24 aoxypoves. Herwerden should
not have conjectured duyjuovos. We are
passing to a fresh point. dpovola and
doxnuoctvn (‘bad form’) tend to duerpia
‘excess,’ ‘extravagance’ in behaviour
(cf. Arist. Zth. Nic. tv 8. 11252 12—16);
and extravagant behaviour is a form of
untruth, because it makes a man appear
what he is not. The love of truth will
therefore save the philosopher from self-

assertion and bad manners.

‘Politics cannot be taught’ was (in Plato’s
view) the theoretical basis of Athenian
political life: see Prot. 319 A—320 D.
We are here invited to suppose that it
was actually maintained in so many
words by sophists, demagogues, and
others.. Something of the sort is asserted
by Isocrates adv. Soph. 14, 213 but it is
unlikely that Plato is alluding to Isocrates
in particular, as Teichmiiller supposes
(Zit. Fehd. 1 p. 104).

Plato constantly did. éroluwous xataréu-
vew admirably expresses the vindictive
fury of the insulted demagogues, but
should not be taken as an allusion to
Socrates’ fate. Plato felt his master’s
death too deeply to exaggerate on such
a subject. See VII 517 A7.

23 8s dv EvANapBdvew KrA. Jack-
son suggests that Isocrates is intended

488D 25 émalovres. I should adopt
the accusative with Stallbaum and others
were it not for oléuevor. Schneider is
fully justified in saying ‘‘si Plato éwatovres
scripserat et oldmevor, fieri vix poterat,
quin prius vitiosum quibusdam et in
accusativum mutandum videretur: al-
terum ipsa distantia tutum erat.” This
is precisely what has happened, for while
g (with some other Mss, but not II or &)
has ératovras, all the Mss, without excep-
tion, have olduevor. The anacoluthon is
not harsher than other instances in which
the best mss have the nominative of the
participle instead of the accusative, e.g.
Phaedy. 241 D, Soph. 219 E, Laws 885 D,
Phaed. 81 A. See also Classen on Thuc.
II 53. 4, where many parallel instances
are quoted from Thucydides. A long and
unperiodic sentence like the present is
peculiarly liable to anacolutha: and one
has occurred already in 488c. For these
reasons I now agree with Schneider and
others that the text is sound. The nomi-
natives melOovres and Biasduevot may have
suggested the change to Plato: “WéyovTas
propter émawwodvras tenuit, mox velut
impatiens tenoris diu servati paullisper
de via deflexit’? (Schneider). Similar

ungrammatical anacolutha are found oc- |

casionally also in Inscriptions, when the
sentence runs to a considerable length:
see Meisterhans? pp. 203, 205-

28 Sres 8... KvPepvyticyvy. The
sailors, Plato has already told us, have
not the smallest idea that the true pilot
must study the year and the seasons etc.,
if he is to be truly qualified to rule a ship
(that is to say, from Plato’s point of view,
if he is to know how to steer), but as for
jow he shall steer—let people wish him
to or no—of zhad they think it impossible

to acquire either art or study and there-
with (lit. ‘at-once-and’) the art of steers-
manship. We may translate the sentence
thus: ‘but art or system of ow to steer,
let alone whether people wish him to
steer or no—that they think it impossible
to acquire, and therewithal the art of
steering.’ The true pilot, according to
Plato, is one who knows ow to steer.
Whether others wish him to steer or no,
is wholly irrelevant; see Pol. 293 ff,
where this principle is declared to be of
universal application, and illustrated as
follows from the case of doctors: arpovs

helm into their hands (488 Cc): how to
handle it, they know not, and deny that

AaBelv). What of dua Kal ryv KuBepyy-
tuhv? These words should be taken
closely with what goes before. The literal
translation is (to acquire) ‘at once and
the art of steering’: cf. PAz/. 22 D 6 Blos
obros yéyovev alperos dua kat ayadds ‘this
life is at once choiceworthy and good.’
Now ‘to acquire at once the art of how
to steer (rws KuBepyfjoe, Tovrou Téxvny)
and the art of steering’ is merely a way
of saying ‘to acquire the art of how to
steer and therewith the art of steering.’
He who learns the art and study of how
to steer necessarily Jearns therewith the
art of steering (‘‘quarum qui compos factus
sit, simul gubernatoriam artem teneat”’
Schneider): for kuBepyyrex7 is, according
to Plato, simply and solely the art of Zow
to steer. Ti KuBepyyrixiy, in short, is
nothing but the réxvn and pedéry Tovrou
dws KuBepyfoer, expressed from Plato’s
point of view. Thus in denying that it
is possible to learn either réxvy or medéry
of how to steer, the sailors are in effect
emphatically denying that it is possible
to learn xuSepyntixh in Plato’s sense of
the word at all: cf. 488 B @dokovres unde
didaxrdv elvat. So much for the meaning
of this passage as a whole. In regard to
details, it should be noted that 67ws means
Show’: ‘éaws ad rovrov spectans modum
et rationem potius quam finem significat’
(after Schneider). With Schneider also
I understand «uBepyjcer as ‘shall steer’
and not ‘shall get possession of the helm.”
medérn is ‘study’ (in the more concrete

sense of the word), rather than actual
exercise or practice: cf. III 402 B gore rhs
avris Téxvns Te Kal mehérns. With réxvqv
AaBetv cf. Pol. 300 E.

The above explanation agrees in the
main with that of Schneider, and is in
my opinion what Plato meant to say.
For other views see App. I.

10 Tovs pt) xpopévovs. Those who

12 rods cohovs kth. “The learned
pate Ducks to the golden fool” ( Zimon of
‘Athens IV 3). See also VIII 568 Aw.
The author of the saying was, according
to Aristotle (Ret. 11 16. 13919 8 ff.),
Simonides. Being asked on one occa-
sion by Hiero’s queen whether it was
better to be a man of genius (copés) or
rich, he replied ‘‘Rich; for men of genius
are found at the court of the rich’”—a
characteristic reply, by which the court-
liest of ancient poets contrived to flatter
the queen without forgetting himself. (It
should be remembered that copés often
means ‘poet’.) There is no reason for
supposing (with e.g. Teichmiiller Zzz.
Fehd.1 p. 102) that Plato attributed the
saying to Aristippus, although a witticism
on the subject is ascribed both to him
(D. L. 1 8. 69) and to Antisthenes
(Winckelmann Antisth. Frag. Pp. 58).
Plato liked to get his knife into Simo-
nides: see I 331 E ff.

489 16 detcOar is governed by the
idea of obligation carried on from avary-
xatov. The alternative suggested by J.

See

and C., that the infinitive depends on
mépuxev, is impossible.

20 év rovrots. rovrors is neuter, like
roUrwy: otherwise trd—émirndevdyTwy is
hardly necessary. The balance of clauses
—‘in consequence of these circumstances,
and amid these circumstances’—is also in
favour of this—Schneider’s—view.

489 c—491 A So much for the ‘use-
lessness’ of the philosopher. But the most
serious prejudice from which Philosophy
suffers is owing to those who pretend to be
philosophers when they are not. It is they
who are meant, when people assert that
the majority of philosophers are depraved.
Let us endeavour to shew that Philosophy
is not responsible for the corruption of the
philosophic nature. The true philosopher,
in spite of popular misconceptions, ts, as
we have seen, naturally a lover of Ti ruth,
and therefore possesses all the virtues of
character already named. We have to
enquire (1) how this disposition becomes in
many cases depraved and (2) what is the
character of the false philosophers who are
responsible for the prejudice against Phito-
sophy.

489 © 31 éxetOev—d0ev. As dva-
pupy}okouae takes the genitive of a noun,
so it can be followed by a genitival—

32 Kaddv te kdyaldy. The fashion-
able Greek phrase adds kdyafds for an
é\evOépios, or gentleman, was continually
used by Socrates and his followers to
express their ideal of what a man should
be. An excellent discussion of the So-
cratic connotation of the word will be
found in Doring Die Lehre des Sokrates
pp. 398—415: for its usual implica-
tions reference may be made to Schmidt
Ethik d. alten Griechen 1 pp. 328—334.
In politics, the expression was applied to
the wealthy or oligarchical party (cf. VIII
569 A and Thuc. vill 48.6). It is there-
fore probable that Socrates’ habitual use of
xadds xayabés fostered the not unwar-
ranted suspicion that he and his friends
were out of sympathy with democracy,
and so contributed in some measure to
his condemnation and death.

he had asserted before (485 A—C) that
the philosopher is a lover of 7d ov. ety
(‘ was,’ ie. ‘is, as we saw’) would be the
‘ philosophic imperfect’ in direct speech,
For the rare change from 7 to ely after
a primary tense cf. Xen. Mem. 1 2. 34
Sfrov (sc. early) bre dgexréov etn Tod
ép0as Aéyew, where ely stands for 7
(the usual ‘erat’ for ‘esset’ with words
denoting obligation or necessity) of the
direct, and Plato Charm. 156 B déyouat
mov Ore odx oldy Te avToUs mdvous Emixerpely
rovs 6pOadpods lacOat, GAN’ avarykatoy ety
dpa Kal rhy Kepadhy Oeparevew. (Mad-
vig’s insertion of dy after dyaykatoy in
this passage is without authority.) Cf.
also 11 361 #. The sequence is all the
more easy with the philosophic imperfect
because its very nature involves a refer-
ence to the past. Ast’s conjecture drre-
oynodue0a is incorrect ; for the philo-
sopher’s zeal for Being has not yet been
urged in defence of the statement—now

4908 11 &vyyevet. vois is akin to
Being and the Eternal: cf. Phaed. 79 D,
Tim. 90 A—C and infra X 611 E.
 wAnoidcas KTA.: ‘whereby having
come nigh unto and married with true
Being, begetting Reason and Truth, he
attained unto knowledge and enjoyed
true life and nourishment, and then but
not before ceased from travail of the soul.’
The mystic union of the Soul with Being
is here described in passionate and glow-
ing language. Cf. Phaedr. 246 E—247 D,
Symp. 210 A—212 A, and many parallels
in Plotinus, for whom, as for the Neo-
platonists generally, the mystic side of
Platonism had an extraordinary fascina-
tion: see Zeller? 111 2, pp. 611—618.

The imagery should be compared with
Theaet. 156 A ff. where the phenomena
of Perception are thus analysed. The
Subject unites with the Object, and from
this union are born two children, one
the ato@nots e.g. Sight, the other the
aloOnrév e.g. 7d wéhav. The former be-
longs more peculiarly to the Subject, the
latter to the Object. Similarly with the
phenomena of Knowledge. The Subject
unites with the Idea, and the children of
this union are vods (or rather, strictly
speaking, véyous i.e. the aclzon of vos),
on the side of the Subject, and the vonréy,
i.e. Truth, on the side of the Object. We
miss an essential point if we take vody
as the object of Knowledge; it is the
faculty of Reason, no longer dormant,
but suddenly called into actuality. Plato
means that Reason does not really live
until it lays hold on the Idea. -yvoln cor-
responds to vodv; it is by the begetting ot
vous that we come to know. The aorist
denotes the instantaneous act; cf. Sy.
210 E mpds Tédos Hin lov—étaipyns
karbWerat te Oavpacrov Thy piow Kaddv
xTv. See also on 508 D and cf. VII 517 C.
In like manner dAn0Gs {wn balances
ddnGeay: there is no true life without
knowledge of the Truth. ad7@dés goes
also with tpépocro: cf. Phaedr. 247 D and
248 B,C. With &dtvos cf. Phaedr. 251 E
(ddivew ntev) and Symp. 206 E. It is
tempting to suppose that in wdivos Plato
is thinking not merely of the lover’s pangs,
but also of the pangs of birth. The
knowledge of the Idea is indeed in
Plato’s view an intellectual and moral
regeneration, But daodiyyou ro épwros
shews that @Sivos means the throes of
love; and the further view introduces a
confusion of ideas which is alien to the
peculiar character of Plato’s ‘mysticism.’

19 dvaykdtovta has been doubted.
It is read by all the Mss except &, which

dvapiBatovra. The textis perfectly sound.

dueAnrvbévar. J. and C.’s translation

curate here.

490D 23 daly. In 487 c we have
galy dv ris, and Richards would add dp
here. But the hypothetical critic (with
whom Socrates himself agrees 487 E) is
now treated as what he really is—the
exponent of opinions held by all. We
should translate ‘after you objected that
all men would be compelled to agree
with what we say, but when they set
words aside, and looked at the actual
people of whom the argument spoke,
they declared that’ etc. For daly after
dvaykacOyjcerat (rather than dvayxacO%-
gotro) see Kiihner Gr. Gr. 11 p. 1061.

24 THs StaPodys. IL and the majority
of MSS have 77s 759 dtaBorjs. If 76n is
right, it must, I think, be taken with rs
dvaBod7js in the sense of ‘ea diaBory quae
iam apparebat et in conspectum venerat,
cum antea animadversa non fuisset’ (so
Bernhardy and Schneider Adazt. p. 46).
Even so, it is harsh, but not so harsh as
if we take it with émvcxomovvTes, as Stall-
baum—and formerly Schneider—did. So
extreme a hyperbaton would be more
difficult than that in Soph. O. 7, 1245,
and scarcely admissible in prose. Perhaps
Plato wrote Tis duaBodjs 76n. Otherwise
we must suppose that A and other mss
are right in omitting the word.

490E 31 TAs pipovjévas kTA. Plato
distinguishes between two kinds of rovnpia,
that which results from the corruption of
the truly philosophic nature, and the o-
vnpla of pretenders to philosophy. It
is the latter—so we are told—which is
responsible for the prejudice under which
Philosophy labours (cf. 489 D): but the
former is by far the more serious evil
(491 E, 495 B), though engendered, not
by Philosophy, but by the seductive in-
fluence of public opinion.

491A—495B The philosophic nature
zs a rare growth, whose very virtues render
it peculiarly liable to corruption, when tt
is placed in unfavourable surroundings.
The clamorous voice of public opinion,
expressed in assemblies and other gather-
ings of the people, inevitably corrupts the
youth by moulding them into conformity
with itself. Where necessary, force ws
employed, under the name of punishment,
Against these influences, no teacher can
possibly contend, although the providence
of God may save some. As for the
Sophists, they do but make into a system
and teach the opinions of the Multitude,
which they are wholly unable to justify,
but accept without reserve, as their pro-
fession requires them to do. Remember
too that the Ideas are foolishness to the
Many, so that they will never love Wisdom
or her followers. Socrates concludes with

A. P, I.

avivid and lifelike picture of a philosophic
nature in process of corruption.

12 amédAvor KTA. Krohn (Pl. Sé.
p. 114) asks how courage and temperance
can tend to corrupt the character. The
answer is given by Plato in 494 B ff.
They bring their possessor to the front,
and therefore expose him to the solicita-
tions of selfish and unscrupulous men. It
should be carefully borne in mind that
dyépela and the other virtues are here
regarded, not as the result of education,
but as natural qualities, derived from the
philosopher’s native love of truth. We
are in fact dealing with the fotentiality of
the rehéws giddcogos (491 A). It is this
which suffers corruption, not the actual-
ized philosopher. Cf. Krohn l.c. p. 115
and Pfleiderer Zur Losung etc. p. 26.

a9lc 17 es yap—Acyo: not
‘now I have given you an outline of my
meaning” (D. and V.), but ‘you under-
stand the general type of the things I
mean,’ that is, the general character of
things which @0elpe. kal droomg, though
in themselves advantages or even virtues.
Adimantus assents, but would like to
have them specified more precisely. In
reply, Socrates bids him grasp the notion
of them correctly as a whole (avrod is
neuter and édou airod is practically equi-
valent to 7tUmov), and it will become
clear to him, and 7a mpoewpynuéva mepl
abrév (viz. that they dardAdvot Kat doom
— pbelper kai droomg B, C) will zot appear
roma as before (&romov—dxodoa in B).
The passage is somewhat loosely written ;

but airy is certainly neuter and not
‘the philosophic natures,’ as J. and C.
suppose. :

491D 21 éyyewv—tdov. These are
possessive genitives. Richards says that
“Gv should probably be omitted before
¢gwv or added before éyyelwv.” Cf. how-
ever IV 438. In this instance I think
Plato wrote r&v ¢gwy in order to call
special attention to {Ga as opposed to
éyyea. They are not on the same level
of importance, as far as the argument is
concerned, for it is the degeneration of
tga, not of éyye, which Plato has to
explain.

23 mAeusvev is much more elegant than
Madvig’s conjecture m)eZov. Plato’s po-
sition on this matter, in the way in which
he states it, is open to objection. It
might be argued that the naturally strong
nature is the best fitted to resist the
corrupting influences of its environment.
But the philosophic nature is remarkable
for sensibility as well as strength, and
the sensitive plant needs careful fostering.
The general sentiment of this passage is
Socratic, as Hermann (Gesch. u. System
Pp. 330 #. 33) and Krohn (77%. St. p. 365)
have pointed out: cf. Mem. IV 1. 3, 4 Ta

25 tiv aploryy diow «tA. The
contrast is between the aplorn gvots and
the gatdn, where oth are subjected to

former ‘comes off worse,’ ‘suffers more’
(kdxwoy dmradddrrev), because the tpod7)
is more alien to zs nature than to that of
the others: ef. rocotrw mredvwv évdet THv
mpendvrwy. So Schneider correctly ex-
plains the passage. Cf. generally Dante
inferno V1 106—r108 ‘*Ritorna a tua
scienza, Che vuol, quanto la cosa é pit
perfetta, Pit senta ‘1 bene, e cosi la

491 = 30 é veavixys KTA.: “out of
a fulness of nature ruined by education”
Jowett. Plato’s attitude on this subject
is highly characteristic. The educator’s
whole efforts are to be directed towards
saving and improving strong and gifted
natures: cf. VII 519 A, B- Weak natures
may be almost neglected, without serious
injury to the State. They will never do
anything great—whether good or evil:
see 495 B. For this and other reasons
Plato does not trouble to lay down rules
for the education of the lower classes in
his city.

492A 3 pi &v TpooHKOvoy: ig. &v

uy tmpoonkoton (which Stephanus wrongly
read), by acommon hyperbaton: cf. Crito
47 D and other examples in Braun De
Hyperb. Pl. p. 15. With mpoonkovoy it
is usual to supply waOjoe. I think Plato
intentionally selects a vague expression,
intending év mpoonxovey to be taken with
orapetca—gurevocioa as well as with
tpépnrat: for it is just as important that
the philosophic nature should be sown
and planted in a proper soil (491 D), as
that it should receive proper education.
Morgenstern, who formerly proposed mpoc-
nkovon <yn>, afterwards adopted much
the same view as this: see Schneider
Addit. p. 46.

5 Oedv. See on Oeod potpay 493 A.

ty Kal od qyet KTA. This passage is
appealed to by Grote (VIII pp. 200 ff.)
in his famous defence of the Sophists.
Plato certainly implies that the Sophists
did not independently corrupt the young
‘to any extent worth mentioning’ (6 7
kal d&&ov Adyou). It is the Demos which
is the primary source and fount of cor-
ruption ; the Sophists are only the mouth-
piece of a disgraceful public opinion
which it is their profession to flatter and
court (493 A—D). But from Plato’s point
of view this is itself a sufficiently grave
indictment to bring against a professional
teacher of Morality (see 493 C), so that
the present attack on the Athenian people
is far from being an apology for the
Sophists.

18 molay dv. I agree with Goodwin
(MT. pp. 66, 68, 71) and others that dv
with the future was occasionally used by
the best Attic prose writers. In Plato it
occurs Af. 29 C, 30B, Symp. 222 A, Rep.
X 615 D, Crilo 53D, Euthyd. 287 D,
Phaedy. 224 8, and probably also else-
where. All these instances have been
‘emended,’ and it is possible enough that
some of them are corrupt. Here dp is in
all the mss, and is therefore better re-
tained, although it may of course be an
erroneous repetition of the last syllable of
motay (as Cobet and others suppose). We
may regard the idiom as one of Plato’s
numerous half-poetical efforts: see xX
618 D#. Richards proposes 67: but see
V 450C%.

rov II: 7d A.

flood.) In this there is nothing but the
common passage of a relative into a main
sentence Le II 357B. and cf. dp. 40A
with my note ad loc.), coupled with an
easy change of subject, as in Crzto 46 A.
The sentence assumes the form which it
has in the text, because both subordinate
and main clauses can take the accusative
with infinitive in Greek oratio obliqua:
see Kiihner Gy. Gr. 11 p. 1056. Stallbaum
(followed by J. and C.) understands ov«
oer to account for djcewv, but the nega-
tive cannot be supplied, and if it could,
it would give a wrong sense. Schneider’s
translation is correct, but not his note in
the text. With the sentiment cf, Gorg.
510 D ff.

26 @avdrots. The fate of Socrates
was the most conspicuous example of
this in Plato’s time, but it is hardly
likely that Plato is specifically alluding
to it here (as Steinhart and Susemihl
suppose). The description is quite
general. Contrast VII 517 A 2.

4925 30 ovteydp KTA. Plato has
just declared that it would be the height
of folly in a teacher even to attempt to
make a young man run counter to public
opinion. The present sentence explains
why. ‘There is not, never has been, and
never will be produced a character different
{from the Many) in respect of virtue, by
having been educated on principles op-

posed to the education which the Many
provide (the force of public sentiment,
expressed in assemblies etc.). Conse-
quently every attempt to produce such
a character by means of education in the
teeth of public opinion is foredoomed to
failure. The statement appears at first
sight extraordinary; but from Plato’s
oint of view it is, with the limitations
which he makes, strictly correct. Cities
are either actual or ideal. In the ideal
city, education does not produce a type
of character which conflicts with public
opinion, because public opinion is itself
formed by education. In actual cities,
education must conform to the same
standard if it is to exist at all: for tov
ph tweObuevov driplars re Kal xpjuace Kal
Oavdrows Koddgovct (492 D). How then
are we to explain the presence of great
and good men in existing cities? They
are Geto dvdpes, saved from corruption by
grace of God: see on 493 A. In these
circumstances, what is the political re-
former to do? He must break with all
existing cities (497 8B), and found—as
Plato now wishes to do—a new common-
wealth in which sound education and
public opinion no longer differ, but
agree. In other words, his policy must
be to make the Philosopher King. For
other views of this passage see App. II.

on the proverb 76 Oelov ap Néyou, for
which cf. Symp. 176 C Zwxpdrn 8 é&aipd

rbyou, Phaedr. 242 B Syuplayv OnBatov
Earp Adyou (a delicate way of hinting

a93A 1 Qcod potpav is best ex-
plained by Men. 94B ff. and 99 C, D.

Distinguished statesmen like Pericles,
Themistocles etc. are efor, just as much
as the xpnouwool, wdvrecs, and movntikol :
they are émlavo.—xal Karexbpevor éx TOD
Geo0, drav KaropO&ct AéyorTes TOANG Kab
peydra mpdyuara (99 D). Education did
not produce them, nor have they any
scientific knowledge of statesmanship;
for which reason also they cannot teach
their sons to be statesmen (AZen. 94 B,
Prot. 320 A). It was by this theory that
Plato accounted for the fact that good
men appear from time to time even in
corrupt States: elol yap év rots modXois
dvOpwmror det Oetol rwes, ob mo\dol— dub-
prevot obSér [.Gdov év ebvomoupévass wodeow
H kal ph (Laws 951 B). There is more
than a touch of irony in the epithet @etos
when Plato applies it to Themistocles,
Pericles and other successful politicians
with whom he had little sympathy, but
Ge00 poipay is not ironical here (cf. 492 A),
nor is Plato ever otherwise than grateful

for the birth of statesmen who are truly
@ciou. But they do not solve the difficulty,
for the scientific knowledge of woNir¢x7) is
not only better and more stable in itself,
but guarantees the permanent prosperity
of a State, because it can be transmitted
to posterity. Nor can we be sure that
our statesmen ‘by grace of God’ will
appear when they are most wanted. For

493c 16 évopator KTA.: ‘employs
all these terms in accordance with’ (liter-
ally ‘in dependence on’) ‘the opinions of
the mighty Beast.’ This interpretation
is better than to suppose with Stallbaum
that Plato means ‘applies all these names
to the opinions’ etc., though dvoudfew Te
ézi tw is idiomatically used in that way.

19 Tavaykaia—Kahot. TavaryKaia does
not mean “the physical necessities and
exigencies of the great beast’s nature
(J. and C.), but simply ‘the inevitable.’
Whatever happens, a public teacher or
Sophist must conform to the opinions
of the Beast (492 D). In what follows
there is a hint of the profound philo-
sophical view that the Works of Necessity
are evil (cf. Zim. 29 E, 47 Eff.), and that
Moral Freedom consists in following
what is good. See on X 6175

statesman -or the like, he ‘makes the
Many his masters more than is necessary.’
In a private station, he is, comparatively
speaking, independent; but even then
the Many are (in a certain sense) of
necessity his masters: see 496D. Ast and
Stallbaum take the phrase with 7 Avo-
phdera dvdyKn. Tungenda sunt verba sic:
avdryxn (éoTlv) atte mépa Tay dvayKalwy
(ultra necessaria quae progrediatur) 7 Aco-
phdera Aeyouevy, ut vocabulis 7 Aeyouévn
Aowhdeta istud répa dvaryKkatov declaretur”
(Stallbaum). If this is what Plato meant,
he expresses it in a harsh and dangerously
ambiguous way, and it would be prefer-
able to cancel mépa ray dvayKatov (with
Cobet and Herwerden). But there is
fortunately no occasion for such drastic
treatment.

28 1 AvopySeaxrd. Most of the Mss
write Atoundela (sic), but Avopjderd ye
at the end of a line in Ar. Zccl. 1029
makes it clear that the word is pro-
paroxyton, unless, as Schneider supposes
(Addit. p. 47), Aristophanes shortens the
final syllable by poetic license. The
proverb, which is used of an overmaster-
ing necessity, is illustrated by Leutsch
u. Schneidewin Paroem. Gr. 1 p. 59%
11 p. 367,'and also by Blaydes on Ar. lc.
Two explanations of it were given. Ac-
cording to the first, which is adopted by
the Scholiast on this passage, the phrase

originated in the treatment meted out by
Diomede to Odysseus, when they were
returning from Ilium to the Greek camp
after stealing the Palladium. Odysseus
attempted to kill Diomede, but failed,
and Diomede paid him out by tying his
arms together and driving him home with
blows from the flat of his sword. The
Scholiast on Ar. l.c. explains differently.

6 000s tmmous dvOpwropdryous clrev. I
agree with Schneider that the proverb is
more likely to have originated from the
first story than from a euhemeristic ex-
planation of the man-eating mares of
Diomede of Thrace. ;

tained by Schneider, who takes it as

14 tmokeloovrar=‘they will lie pro-

494c 17 édv tuxy KTA. It has
long been admitted that this picture is
drawn chiefly from Alcibiades. In an-
tiquity Plutarch seems to have suspected
something of the sort, for he describes
Alcibiades’ degeneration in language
adapted from the present passage (A/c.
“4. 1). But the personal touches must
not blind us to ‘the fact that Plato is

sits for the portrait.

18 mAovcovos—péyas describe Alci-
biades exactly: cf. Alc. I 104 A, B, Thuc.
v1 16 1—3, Isocr. epi fedyous 25 ff., Plut.
Alc. 1. 4, 4. 1 and elsewhere. The Greeks
thought tallness essential to beauty: see
e.g. Arist. th. Nic. IV 7. 1123> 7 and
Pol. Vil 4. 13267 33.

19 tyyobpevov KTA. Plutarch (A/c. 17.
2, 3) declares that Alcibiades intended
the Sicilian expedition to be a step
towards an almost universal empire :
Sicily was to be merely the épédca rod
modguov. Alcibiades says nearly as much
himself in Thuc. vi 90. 2, with which
compare 15. 2. Grote (VII p. 79) is
inclined to deny that even Alcibiades
dreamt of anything beyond the conquest
of Sicily, but the ancient historians
thought differently: cf. also Ad. II
141 B ff Many of the Athenians,
probably not without reason (though
Plutarch l.c. 35. 1 leaves the point
unsettled), suspected him of aiming at
a tupavvis (Thuc. VI 15. 4 and Isocr.
mept fetryous 38).

an extract from some tragic poet (prob-

from the rhythm (oxnpario wod—Kev od)

23 ovr Srariepéve: not “while he is
in this frame of mind” (D. and V.), but

édy tis xrd. As Socrates often did

24 Oo 8€ is not here the idiomatic 76

27 To Evyyevés KTA.? ie. the affinity of
what is said with his nature, “weil die
Reden mit ihm verwandt sind” (Schnei-
der). D. and V.’s translation ‘‘an inborn
taste for philosophic inquiry ” is wrong.

eis has often been doubted; but
Schneider’s explanation is certainly right,
that 7G otrw diaridepévy is the individual
typifying a class, and that els denotes ome
of the class. The idiom is analogous to
the plural after a typical or generic
singular: see on I 347 A. The emenda-
tions proposed (dia Odynrae for efs alcOd-
vara Stallbaum, elcavéis Richter, elow
Madvig, eloaxotwy or eloakovoas Richards)
are not only superfluous, but indefensible
in themselves. Plato hardly expects more
than one such person to pause at all on
his downward career. Here again we
naturally think of Alcibiades, whose
interviews with Socrates (according to
Symp. 215 D) profoundly impressed him
for the moment, but failed to effect a

permanent reform in the midst of so
many temptations (ib. 216 B). Perhaps
Socrates once hoped that Alcibiades
would be his ‘scientific ruler,’ and bring
back true prosperity to Athens. A tone
of sorrow for the ‘lost leader’ seems to
make itself felt in Plato’s words.

4945 29 Tovs youpévous: i.g. ot
fryoovrat, whence the article, which Her-
werden wrongly rejects. The voice should
pause a little between éxetvous (which
refers to 494) and Tous Hyoupevous.

30 déyovrds Te KT. We should ex-
pect the future indicative, and on this
ground the insertion of Siaredeiv has been
proposed by Richards (Stephanus had
previously desiderated Suareddsew). So
serious an alteration lacks every element
of probability. Ast must be wrong in
making Aéyovras etc. depend on olducba.
If the text is sound, we should supply
mévra Spdcew or the like after ov, and
regard the participles as agreeing with
the subject of dpacew. (Schneider and
J. and C. take nearly the same view).
dpdcew is of course easy to understand,
but it is less easy to dispense with mavra.
Could Plato have written ob <mav>, way
pev épyov krh.? Cf. IX §75E and may
moi in Ap, 39 A and Gorg. 479 © I
prefer the anacoluthon.

31 Tov welGovTa. Such was Socrates,
and he was brought to trial. Plato may
well have thought of his master when
he wrote Snuoclg els d-yavas Kadiordvras.
The most fatal count in the charge against
Socrates was that he corrupted the youth
(Ap. 24 B), and Alcibiades was held to
be a case in point (Xen. Mem. I 2. 12).
Plato now turns the tables on the Athenian
people. He says in effect ‘It was you
who corrupted Alcibiades: and you im-
peached Socrates for trying to save him.’

495D 23 & Tay TEXVoV ktA. It
has been supposed that Plato has in view
Antisthenes and the Cynic Diogenes, the
latter of whom apparently started life as a
moneychanger (D. L. VI 20). But the de-
scription which follows applies to sophists
and sophistical rhetoricians rather than to
the Cynic philosophers. The poet Gray
says ‘this seems to be aimed at Protagoras,
who was an ordinary countryman and a
woodcutter”’ (see Gellius Woct. Att. V 3
and other authorities cited by Frei Quaest.
Prot. pp. 6ff.). Hermann (Gesch. u. Syst.
p.628) cites Euthydemus and Dionysodorus
as cases in point (cf. 496 A”.). Each of
these sophists had formerly taught the art
of fighting in full armour (E£uthyd. 27. C—
242 B, 273 £). As speech-writing and
rhetoric generally were counted among
the arts, we may think also of Isocrates,
who loved above everything to call him-
self a girdcopos (Antid. 271 ff.). But
although these and other examples may
be quoted in illustration of what Plato
here says, the tone of the whole passage
shews that Plato is describing a familiar
phenomenon of his own times, when
clever and ambitious young men were in
the habit of forsaking their handicrafts
and deyoting themselves to ‘culture.’ Cf.
Prot. 318E Tas yap TéExvas adrovs mepev-
yoTas GKovTAas advw ab yovres euBar-
Rovow els téxvas (sc. Hippias etc.),
oytcpods TE Kal dorpovoplay Kar yewpe-
Tptav Kal povorkyy ddaoKovTes, and my
article in C/. Rev. XV Pp. 220.

27 ébrépevor KTA. 1S an anacoluthon.
The natural flow of the sentence is inter-
rupted by the question 7 ovK avayKn }
which is intended to obtain Adeimantus’
assent to Tas Wuxas—Tuyxdvovow. On

resuming, Plato interposes a comparison,
and to this the general idea which forms
the logical predicate to 7roNQol is accom-
modated in ot?’ drra—gaitdra. The sen-
tence was thus understood by the editor
of g; for Tuyxdvoucw, which seems a
difficulty on this theory, is in g TVYXa-
vovres. But tuyxdvovres would be ex-
tremely inelegant; and Plato writes
tvyxdvousw to correspond to AcAwByvTat.
Even in other cases a finite verb some-
times replaces a participle in the second
of two contrasted clauses, e.g. 4p. 21 E.
J. and C. explain the passage in nearly
the same way, as well as (apparently)
Schneider and Stallbaum. It is im-
possible for many reasons to connect
Tuyxdvovow with édrémevor and so escape
the anacoluthon. I formerly suspected
the text, and proposed <dro>rTvyxd-
vovow (‘miss the mark,’ ie. fail to win the
distinction which they covet). Another
solution might be to place the troublesome
rvyxdvovow after moddol, But neither
change is in any degree probable; and it
is better to acquiesce in the reading of the
Mss. Plato’s anacolutha are a device for
imparting life and reality to his dialogues.
A careful translation should preserve
them all.

6é cwudtrov Onruvopévav Kal at puxal
mond appwordrepar ylyvovra. It is pro-
bable that Bavavola was ‘primarily a
military conception, dependent for its
origin on the obvious fact that certain
modes of life and the exercise of certain
trades disqualify from prowess in the
field” (Greenidge Gk. Const. History p. 22,
quoting in support Hdt. 1 165—167).
“ Sedentary and within-door arts,” says
Bacon (quoted by Newman Politics of
Aristotle 1 p. 105), “have in their nature
a contrariety to a military disposition.”
In practice the term is freely applied by
the writers of the best period to every
kind of mechanical or illiberal labour or
pursuit. Aristotle defines Bavavola in
these words: Bdvavoov 5’ épyov elvan det
rotro voultew Kal réxvnvy ratrny Kal
pdOnow, boar mpos Tas xphoes Kal Tas
mpdées Tas THs dperfhs dxpnotov amepyd-
fora 7d oGpya Tov édevdépuy 7 THY
yoxiv @ rhv didvoay (Pol. © 2. 1337”
8 ff.). See also Whibley Gk. Olzg. pp.
42 ff. and Newman l.c. pp. 104—II5.
The ancients mostly derived the word
from Badvos ‘a furnace’ and aiw, “quasi
Bdvavoos qui caminum accendit”’ (Ste-
phanus-Hase Zes.s.v.). In view of the
Boeotian Bavd=yur7y and Bavijxas* yuvaixas
Bowwrol in Hesychius, I have conjectured in
Cl. Rev. vil p. 112 that Bdvavoos, which
does not look like an Attic word, may be
connected with Bavd. If so, the word
perhaps originally meant © effeminate’

‘unmanly’: cf. Oydvvouévwy in the ex-
tract quoted from Xenophon. In any
case, however, the ancient etymology can
hardly be right.

31 Soxeis ody tu «TA. In the ‘little
bald tinker’ several critics have recognised
Isocrates: see for example Teichmiiller
Lit. Fehd. 1 p. 108 and Jackson’s article
on the Sophists in the Zc, Brit. ék
decuGv Nedumévouv—ev Badravelw dé de-
Novuévov is an admirable example of
rhetorical wapouolwors, and satirises the
tricks of style for which Isocrates was
notorious. But all the  sophistical
rhetoricians of the school of Gorgias
affected meretricious ornaments of this
kind (see Hug on Symp. 194 E ff. and
especially Cope’s Rhetoric of Aristotle
III pp. 105, 106), and Plato’s shafts are
not levelled at Isocrates alone. As usual,
he individualises the type, and if the
resultant picture resembles Isocrates, so
much the worse for him. Plato would
not be sorry (cf. Huthyd. 305 ff., with
Spengel’s Jsokr. u. Pl. pp. 36—40), and
doubtless intended his readers to think of
Isocrates, as they certainly would. See
also on 408 E.

496 A—497A The few who, from
various reasons, remain faithful to philo-
sophy, withdraw from political life. By
so doing, they keep themselves unspotted
from the qworld—no mean achievement, yet
not the greatest. If they meet with a
commonwealth appropriate to them, they
will themselves attain a larger growth, as
well as prove the saviours of their country.

496B 9 imo puyfs KaTadnpoey:
‘arrested by exile,’ so as not éxmeceiy éK
rod émirndedparos (493 A): cf Karacxely
below. Stallbaum’s explanation ‘ over-
taken byexile,’ which D.and V.apparently
accept, isin my judgment wrong. Theread-
ing xatadepbér (g and several other Mss,

followed by Ast and one or two other
scholars) is less expressive and picturesque,
though it gives a fair sense if interpreted as
kararepder TH girooogplg. Van Heusde’s
conjecture awd puyfis Karadepbey would
(as Schneider observes) mean ‘those who
survived after exile’ and is wholly inad-
missible, as well as dd puyfs karahnpev
(‘debarred from exile’), which Herwerden
proposes, inserting also # after 700s. Has
Plato any special instances in view?
Steinhart (Zzndectung p. 208) thinks of
Anaxagoras, and even of Plato himself.
But it cannot be said that either of them
was saved by exile from deserting Philo-
sophy, and Plato was hardly exiled, even
metaphorically speaking. Krohn (7%. <S¢.
pp- 117, 384) declares for Xenophon. It
is however more than doubtful, even
after Boeckh’s attempt to overthrow the
tradition about unfriendliness between
Xenophon and Plato (De simultate quam
Pl.c. Xen. exercuisse fertur 1811), whether
Plato would have gone out of his way to
pay a compliment to his fellow-disciple.
Can Plato be thinking of his friend Dio?
If so, this passage must have been written
in or after 367 B.c., the year of Dio’s
banishment from Syracuse. A personal
reference is easily combined with the
description of a class, and a tribute to
Dio would be very pleasing here. I
have lately found the same conjecture
in Thomas Gray’s notes on the Republic.
See also Jutrod. § 4 and (for Plato’s
connexion with Dio) Grote X pp. 332 ff.
See also 499 B 7.

496 c 18 7d Sapoviov onpetov.
Socrates regarded his divine sign as a
special if not unique revelation from
God, without submitting it to further
analysis. As here, so in 4g. 31 D, it
is this which forbids him to enter on
political life (rodr’ éorw & wou évavriobras
7a modTiKa mpdtrew). What the pheno-
menon really was, is a question which

different writers have answered differently,
according to their different points of view:
see Zeller# 11 1 pp. 75—91- The subject
is treated with great fulness in Ribbing’s
Socrat. Stud. 11 pp. 1 ff. and in Riddell’s
edition of the Aology pp. 109—117. Tor
éumpoobev yéyove does not deny that such
a sign may be vouchsafed to others in
the future. Schneider conjectures that
Plato added this limitation with a view
to himself—an unlikely supposition, al-
though no doubt the present passage is
in some measure intended as a defence of
Plato’s abstention from political life: cf.
490 D2.

eis Onpla dvOpwros KtA. Herwer-
den would read dvOpwmrovs ‘among
men who are as beasts.’ But the point
is that the philosopher in existing com-
monwealths is like a lonely human being
in the midst of wild beasts. Cf. Zzmon
of Athens \v 3 ‘‘The commonwealth of
Athens is become a forest of beasts.”
The comparison may have been suggested
to Plato by Pherecrates’ “Aypro., to which
he alludes in Prot. 327D. In Pherecrates’
play the “Aypto. were savages, to whom
apparently some Athenians betook them-
selves, in the hope of finding more
happiness than they enjoyed in Athens:
see Kock’s Com. Graec. Fr. 1 pp.
146—150. Plato points out that there
are savages enough at home. In méow
dyplos the emphasis is on ma@ow: we
should translate ‘to hold out alone where
all are savages.’ Cf. 7rd wdvTwy modeulov
1X 579 B. Herwerden weakens the
effect by adding ofow after dyplors.

27 AaPev: singular, in spite of yerd-
pevotin 496C. Cf. I 347 A”.

otov év yxelpave KTA. Jowett con-
strues xeudve with Kovioprov; but xewucav
kovioprod for ‘a storm of dust’ is scarcely
a Greek idiom. Kxovioprod—depopévov is
of course a descriptive genitive absolute.

In this way Schneider also took the
passage. dn is ‘tempestuous rain ;
pera, 8uBpov mvo}, as Hesychius explains.
xahdens was once conjectured by Ast,
but he afterwards rightly withdrew the
suggestion. Herwerden proposes ofoy év
(addy Kovioprod bard xrh., and Richards ép
xetmove kal Kovioprod S4dy (or [4X Kovtop-
tod). Neither proposal is supported by
any of the ancient citations, except that
of Themistius (Ov. VIII p. 104 C) whose
reproduction of this passage is in other
respects, as in this, extremely inaccurate:
see Schneider’s note. And (aA kovcoprod
is, to say the least, a questionable phrase.
It is not well to mar the wonderful force
and beauty of writing such as this by
tasteless and inept conjectures, The
passage has often been compared with
Lucretius’ ‘¢ Suave mari magno,” but the
difference is greater than the resem-
blance. The Platonic philosopher is
content (dyad), if he can keep his own
soul pure, because he cannot, as things
now are, save both himself and others.
But it is no pleasure for him to see
‘“‘quibus ipse malis careat,” for he would
fain help others if they would but let
him. That they will not is a misfortune,
not for others only, but for him (av7és
Te paddov avéjoerar KTA.). We seem to
catch in Plato’s words a certain tone of
sorrow, as if he had not himself attained
the highest of which he was capable,
because he could not find a philosophic
city in which to dwell: see Morgenstern
De Plat. rep. p. 161, where reference is
made to 4%. 31 E and to the Platonic
Epistles v 322 A, B, VII 324 B—320 B,
330 C—331 D: cf. also Gorg. 515 A—
522 E, and Susemihl Gen, Az/w. 11 p. 190.

497A 3 ‘mpoonkotons. The only
city suited to the philosophic nature is
Plato’s: cf. IX 892 A.

497 a—498C Our justification of
Philosophy is now complete. It remains
to ask—Where is the political constitution
adapted to the philosophic nature? Where,
in other words, ts the best polity? In the
city which we have founded, except that
the position of the Rulers requires to be
more fully explained. A State, which
zs to handle Philosophy without danger,
must assume a new attitude towards the
subject. Philosophy should receive more,
instead of less attention, as a man grows
older.

Stephanus ought not to have proposed
the insertion of xal before womep: cf. IV
432 D, VIII 557 C and Prot. 311 £,
- where more illustrations are cited in my
note. Stallbaum’s punctuation is here,
I think, preferable to that of Schneider,
who prints only a comma before domep.

certain factor possessed of a reasoned
theory of the constitution, identical with
that possessed by you, the legislator,
when you made the laws.’ The rulers
must understand the constitution and not
merely accept it on the legislator’s autho-
rity, if the spirit of the original legislator
is to survive his death. 6p0H ddfa is not
enough; in order to fill the place of the
founder of the city they require émornun.
Plato confesses that he did not make this
clear enough before (obx ixayGs édnAwOn),
and his confession is most true. In one
passage (IV 429 Cz.), indeed, he seems
expressly to imply that the Rulers do not
fill the legislator’s shoes. But there are
also some hints or traces of the later view :
see on III 414 A, IV 423 E (to which,
perhaps, épp7j0y is intended to refer,
although the reference is hardly justified),
and 442c. Cf. §02 D, 503 A, 504 Dm.

3 ot—arovovpevor: ‘and they it is,
forsooth, who are regarded as accom-
plished in philosophy.’ Sarcasm is often
expressed by an appositional participial
clause at the end of a sentence: cf. e.g.
Ap. 34 4, Crito 51 A. movetoGat here is
the passive of moeiy in the sense of ‘to
construct in fancy,’ ‘represent,’ as e.g. in
Theaet. 197 D and infra Xx 609 Cc. The
usage is at first sight strange, but occurs
again in VII 538 C, IX 573 B and 574 D,
where no other meaning is suitable.
Stallbaum’s attempt to explain the pas-
sages differently is unsuccessful, and the
proposed emendations (such as Ast’s doxov-
pevor for mocovmevor) are unsatisfactory in
each of the four cases. See notes ad ll.

4 éav kal goes with é0é\wow (Schnei-
der) : ‘if they do consent’ (cf. kal amrépuevot
above). It is implied that many, or most
of them, do not.

5 peydAa kTd. Plato distinguishes
three stages. In the first, 7d wept Adyous
is a man’s &pyov after boyhood until he
begins practical life; it then becomes his
adpepyov, and he ‘thinks it great things’
to go to an occasional lecture: towards

old age all but very few neglect philo-
sophy entirely. The proper study of
philosophy reverses all this (wav rovvay-
tlov) as is shewn in B and Cc.

7 vod “Hpaxdetefov Alov. Hera-

losophy.’ Nor is Susemihl (Gen. Zniw. 11

498 c 14 ylyvynrar wth. The
subject is still 7 pdun, ‘their physical
strength,’ not 71s (as J. and C. assert).
In dgérovs véuecOac the metaphor (as
observed by Heindorf on Prot. 320 A)
is taken de gregenuminti alicui consecrato:
cf. Critias 119 D adérwv bvTwy Tadbpwv év
T@ TOO IocedGvos iep@. véwerPa is pro-
perly ‘to graze.’ The effect of the Greek

of course agrees with the subject of vé-
pecOa.

498 c—502 c Adimantus hardly
expects the reasoning of Socrates to carry
conviction to most of his hearers. But
Socrates will not despair, believing that his
words may perhaps bear fruit hereafter,
of not here. As for the Multitude, their
dissent is easily explained. They haveheard
enough of jingling rhetoric, but they have
never yet seen a Philosopher-king, nor
are they accustomed to discourses whose
only aim is truth. Our perfect city ts
realised always and everywhere, whereso-
ever and whensoever Philosophy sits on
the throne. The Multitude will assent,
if we approach them rightly ; for their
hatred is against the false philosophers,
and not against the true. The lover of
Truth is absorbed in contemplation of the
changéless Realities, on the model of which
he will frame human institutions, should

he be called upon to enter public life.
Point this out to the Many, and reason
with them, and they will agree. Our
proposals, though difficult, are not im-
possible.

24 aOus yevdpnevor ‘born again’ im-
plies the re-incarnation of the Soul, as
described in the end of Book x: see on
608 D ff. It is from casual allusions like
the present, made in all seriousness, that
we can best understand how profound
and practical was Plato’s belief in im-
mortality. The seed sown here may bear
its fruit in another life, so that the
educator need not despair.

498E 29 ToT’ arta KTA. Plato
is here alluding to epideictic harangues
by sophistical rhetoricians of the school
of Gorgias. ro.adr’ drra phuara =‘ expres-
sions of this sort’ refers to the jingle in
vyevdmevov—ey bev op, which is an
example of the rhetorical device called
mapomolwors: see Arist. /he?, III 9. 1410*
24 ff. mapopotwors 5° édy duowa Ta eoxara
éxn éxdrepov 7d K@dov, e.g. &v trelaTals
8€ gpovtict Kat ev éaxiorars edict, and
many other examples: see Cope ad loc.
In é£erlrndes—wporwpéva, ‘ assimilated to
one another of set purpose,’ the same
device is meant. Isocrates and his literary
brethren employed it constantly: see the
references On 495 E.

GAN odk—cvprecdvta KTr.: ‘instead
of spontaneously chiming together, as in
the present case’ etc., where the mapo-
polwors of yevouevov and Aeybuevov is
Grd Tod a’roudrov (‘natural,’ ‘sponta-
neous,’ ‘accidental’ as opposed to ée-
aitndes).  Tmaprowpévov and wmorwpévov

are said with a reference to maplowous
(the equality of clauses, as for example in
Isocr. Paneg. 76: see Cope l.c. p. 106)
and mapouolwors. The Many have had
quite enough of maplowors etc. in words ;
but they have never seen a Man srapt-
odbmevov Ti aperp. Plato means that the
time for mincing Rhetoric is past; we want
a Man (&v5paisemphatic),‘‘ with heart head
hand, One still strong maninablatantland,
Whocanrule,and dare not lie” (Tennyson).
It is highly probable, as Diimmler holds
(Chron. Beitr.p.14), that Plato hasspecially
in view Isocrates’ Panegyricus throughout
this passage. The contemptible devices
which Plato here ridicules are extraordi-
narily common in that harangue. For
other views of this passage see App. III.

Tov viv—véory. ‘I do not doubt but
that this was meant as a compliment
and incitement to the younger Dionysius
(see Plato Zpzst. 7, p. 327). And I

understand what follows p. 502 in the

same manner. Hence it seems that this
part of the dialogue was written after
his first voyage to Sicily, and probably
not long before his second, about. Ol.

IoI, 3, when the elder Dionysius was
just dead” (Thomas Gray Works ed.

have taken place just after the old

499C 15 Tovtwy St motepa KrH.
Grote pronounces the Platonic common-
wealth impossible because ‘‘we cannot
understand from whence the force is to
come, tending and competent to gene-
rate” it at the first. Once begun, he
holds, ‘there is no reason why it might
not have continued.” That the real
difficulty is in starting it, Plato himself
clearly understands (cf. sor A n.). He
would not however allow that the diffi-
culty is insurmountable; since a @ela Tis
TUxN (IX 592A) may well occur. But
the true fulfilment of Plato’s Ideal, as
he himself foretells in 1x 592 B, is to
be sought, not in any single earthly
commonwealth, but in its influence,
direct and indirect, upon the moral,
political, religious, and intellectual pro-
gress of mankind: see v 470 Ex”. and
Zeller’s dissertation on Der Platonische
Staat in seiner Bedeutung Sur die Folge-
zeit a ne Vortrigen und Abhandlungen?

78 A. Touches of this kind are rare in

of Plato’s city are not laid in Hellenism,

himself understands the word in 501 B.

10 avrofs and not atrois is certainly
what Plato wrote. False philosophers,
like bad scholars, are always abusing
one another. The middle, which is re-
ciprocal, is also decisive in favour of av-
tos: cf. Charm. 154 A ho.dopoupévous
aXdjAros. J. and C. (with Stallbaum and
the editors generally, except Schneider)
read avrots, remarking that “it was by no
means an uncommon practice of the old
philosophers to abuse the people.” Plato
is not speaking of the old philosophers
at all, but only of sophists and pre-
tenders, who do not abuse, but flatter
and cajole the people (493 4 ff.).

5 ff. and Rhet. 11 4. 1381> 7), or scandal
(Zheaet. 173 D: cf. 174E ff.). His single-

minded pursuit of Truth leaves him
neither time nor inclination to talk about
people.

501B 9 106 pice Slkaroy is assuredly
the Idea of Justice, as opposed to 7d
voum Stkatov ‘conventional justice’: cf.
500C and (for dicet) V 476B. See also
on X 598A. Bosanquet ignores the most
essential and characteristic feature of
Plato’s teaching when he remarks that
“‘the ‘natural’ principles of justice and
other moral qualities are no doubt those
which Plato believes himself to have found
present, in various degrees, throughout in-
organic and organic nature and the animal
world, and culminating in the life of man.”
It is better to let Plato soar where we can-
not follow him than thus to clip his wings.
See the Appendix to Book viI Oz Plato’s
Dialectic.

Io kal mpos éxetvo KTA. The legis-
lative painter looks now at his model
(the pice dtkacoy etc.), now at his picture
(the dixacoy etc. which he is implanting
among men), rubbing out one part and
painting another in again (rd wev—éyypd-
gotev), till he is satisfied with the result.
He combines and mixes various émirndev-
Hara or institutions, till he produces the
true dydpelkehov or ‘colour and like-
ness of true Manhood’; just as the
painter mixes various colours to produce
his dvdpelkedov or flesh-tint. dvdpelxedov
in painting was a sort of flesh-colour,
made by mixing various colours together:
see Crat. 424, Xen. Occ. 10. 5, Arist.
de gen. an. 1 18. 725° 26 and Ruhnken on
Timaeus Lex. s.v. To this Plato of
course alludes, but he intends us also
to take the word in its etymological
signification, as is clear from @eoelkedov
below. The stress in dydpelkedov, as in
Geoedés and Oeoelxedov, is on the first

part of the compound: it is not the mere
avOpwroedés, but the JZan-like, at
which the legislator aims: cf. the force
of dvdpa in 498E. dvdpelxedov might be
translated by ‘the human form divine,’
except that ‘form’ suggests a wrong
notion. For other views on this passage
see App. V.

13 QeoelkeAov. 7.1131 etal. It is
pleasing to meet with so cordial and spon-
taneous an acknowledgment of Homer as
a kindred spirit in a passage so full of
Plato’s characteristic idealism. There is
more than a grain of truth in Longinus’
observation: pévos ‘Hpddoros ‘Ounpixra-
Tos éyévero; Zryatxopos &re wpérepov 6 re
"Apxiroxos, mdvrwy dé TovTwy wddoTa 6
TAdrwv dd 708 ‘Opnpixod xelvov vduaros
els abrov puplas doas maparporas daroxe-
Tevoduevos (rept Upous 13. 3). By the
words 7d dvdpelkeXov—OeoelxeXov Plato
means to suggest that Man is then most
manlike when he most resembles God :
and (as Tennyson says) “‘ then most god-
like being most a man.” Cf. 1x 589 D 2.
This sure and abiding conviction of the
presence of a divine element within us,
rendering our nature essentially and truly
human, makes itself felt in nearly all the
dialogues of Plato. It is the ultimate
source of all his idealism, religious and
metaphysical, no less than moral and
political, and may well be considered the
most precious and enduring inheritance
which he has bequeathed to posterity.

9. audisBynrjce g: dudisByricee AR: duducBynriocews Il.

502A 3 TeTeopévor torwav. See
on 499E. Plato’s attempt to conciliate
the Many is obviously half-hearted. The
Multitude can never be philosophers
(494 A), and are not likely to believe
in the Philosopher-king. But it was
necessary to prove or postulate some
degree of assent or at least quiescence on
their part in order to demonstrate the
possibility of the perfect city. Cf.
502 C 2.

indefinite pronoun is correct.

(Pl. St. p. 125) justly sees in this sen:
tence ‘‘an expression of the convictions
which led Plato to Sicily” in 367 B.c.

499 B x. Richards would read “<oés>
yevouevos or yevouevds <re kal ods yevd-
‘evos> or something similar.” <mepe>
vyevduevos would give the same sense, and
be better Greek. But yevduevos=el yé-
vo.ro, ‘should he arise,’ is sufficient. He

could not be said to be ixavés if he were
corrupted.

14 Gmep tipiv Boel: i.e. the arrange-
ments of Plato’s ka\Atrrodis, as J. and C,
point out. A ruler may arise who will
approve of these, and frame laws accord-
ingly. Plato is trying to prove that his
ideal city is not impossible.

502 c 19 ov pévtor dSivatd ye.
‘Not impossible’ is the final verdict
which Plato’s readers, like Plato himself,
will pass upon his city. His tone is far
less hopeful than in Books 11—1v, and
even in V 473 B ff. he is, I think, more
optimistic. It is impossible not to feel
that 501 C—s02 Cc is written, in some
measure, 2vita Minerva. Plato is glad
to escape from so difficult and uncon-
genial a topic into his native element
again. He is beginning to see that the
Perfect City is in truth a mapddevypa év
ovparg (IX 592B). See on Vv 470k, v1
499, 499E, 502A and VII 540D—
541 B, and cf. Hirmer Ayftst. 2. Komp.
etc. p. 638.

24 ovdev KtA. Cf. 497 C, D mn.
There, as here, the xardoraois Trav dp-
x6vrwy and the position of women and

is ‘Yes, if Philosophers are Kings’; and

the xardoracis Tov dpxdvrwv. Plato is

asserts that the xardoraois Tay dpxdr-

6O3C 15 eipaleisxTA. ‘The faculty
of learning easily, memory, sagacity,
quickness, and so on, together with spirit
and high-mindedness, are, as you know,
not often naturally combined with the
disposition to live soberly in quiet and
stedfast ways’ etc. Plato means that
natural intelligence and vivacity, with
their accompaniments of spirit and
highmindedness, rarely go with moral
stedfastness. A good illustration is
afforded by the contrast between ‘“‘the
Athenian and the Spartan, the former
‘neither resting themselves nor letting
anyone else rest, the latter so slow that
aggression can hardly rouse them to
repel it?” (Bosanquet). Cf. Thucyd. 1
yo. For other views on the text and
interpretation of this difficult passage see
App. VI.

504A 2 dOdors. Seecr. 2. Orelli’s
emendation has met with considerable

J. and C. still retain d\d\os. With the
Ms reading we must, I think, translate
‘in the other cases’ i.e. in the mévo etc.
spoken of just before. (If Plato merely
meant ‘in other kinds of effort’ it was
not worth his while to insert the clause at
all.) But wozep certainly suggests some-
thing more than a mere comparison
between flinching at moral, and flinching
at intellectual tests; and nothing could
be more appropriate, or more in Plato’s
way, than an allusion to the games: see

and the Zaws’” (J. and C.), is scarcely an

504 A—505B Adimantus enquires
what these‘ greatest studies’ are. You will
remember, says Socrates, that we described
our earlier or psychological method of
arriving at the Virtues as inadequate and
imcomplete. Our guardians must travel
by a longer road, tf they would reach their
proper goal, t.e. the highest of all studies,
which ts something above and beyond even
the virtues. And these very virtues must
no longer be seen merely in outline ; they
must be studied in all thetr fulness and
perfection. The highest study ts the Idea
of the Good, as Adimantus has often heard
before. It ts the knowledge of this Idea
which alone renders all other knowledge
useful and profitable.

qv: ‘ut éxpyv et similia dictum”
(Schneider). There is no reason to eject
the word (with Madvig) or to write en:
why should not the mood of the ovatio
recta be retained ? ety would be unpleasing
with etn following so soon. Liebhold’s
ws pev Suvara 7 is unclassical: see on 1

or baov or els TO> Suvardv KdNNCTA KTH.,

to descry them in the best possible way’:

9 ard is of course the four cardinal
virtues, like rafra in 504. D. The ‘longer
circuit’ is the educational training neces-
sary in order to enable the guardians to
obtain scientific knowledge of the virtues
by discerning their relation with the Idea
of Good: cf. 506. In Book Iv Justice,
‘Temperance etc. were regarded as psy-
chological qualities or relations; but the
philosophic Guardians must learn their
metaphysical import. Throughout the
rest of vi and vil Plato, in short,
discards Psychology for Metaphysics.
Thus much is clear; but many difficult
and interesting questions arise in con-
nexion with this passage, as Krohn and
others have pointed out. The paxporépa
meplodos mentioned in IV 435 D appears
to be a longer way of determining, not
the essential nature of the virtues, but
whether Soul has ‘parts’ or not. (A

solution of this difficulty is suggested on
435 D)- Socrates’ shorter road, again, is
not a way by which the Guardians are to
go, but a method employed by himself in
studying primarily the Soul, and second-
arily the virtues. Finally, what is the
relation between the psychological con-
ception of Virtue and the metaphysical?

involve a revised psychology or not?

notes to X 611 B, where Plato himself
appears to raise it. For the last but one
see on 504 D.

the etymological sense of perplws. In

25 Kal petLoy KTA. Kkal—xal=‘ not

ovx troypadyv—Somep viv is as
clear a proof as we could wish that
Justice and the other virtues, as described
in Book Iv, are not the transcendental
avra Kab’ atrd eldn: see on III 402 C.
They are only a broypadh or ‘adum-
bratio’ of the Ideas, being, we may
suppose, simply the psychological rela-
tions which result from the mapovcla of
the Ideas in certain particulars, viz. in
human souls, during their union with
human bodies. doep viv admits more-
over that the Rulers of mr and Iv
(apart from a few suggestions to the
opposite effect: see on 497 C) had only
‘correct opinion’ and not ‘knowledge’
(in the strict Platonic sense): it was their
duty to accept and carry out the precepts
of Plato, the founder of the city (Iv
429 C2.).

word, it is questionable whether Plato
could have used it in this way. Perhaps
we owe the comment to a gratified monk,
who may have applied the observation to
systematic theology. The comment is at
all events a just one. Cf. 496A. Plato’s
remark is best illustrated by the case of
the exact sciences; and in a certain sense
it may be said that he wished to make
Politics into an exact science.

none, if only we take 6 7c not as inter-
rogative, but as the indefinite relative.

épwrds, but the text (‘You may ask it

8. evar Hg: eldévar AIl, sed 6 et € punctis notavit A.

354 D). Some account of the enormous
literature of the subject will be found in
Zeller’ 11 1. pp. 709 ff., 718 2.1. In ad-
dition to Stumpf’s treatise to be presently
named, I have found the monograph by
Biehl Die /dee des Guten bet Platon Graz
1870 particularly good and useful. Other
special treatises are also referred to in the
course of the notes. The majority of
interpreters are now agreed in identifying
Plato’s Idea of the Good with his philo-
sophical conception of the Deity. The
best and fullest proof of the identity is
still, I think, Stumpf’s exhaustive disser-
tation Das Verhiltniss des Platonischen
Gottes zur Idee des Guten Halle 1869.
There is only one passage in his works
where Plato himself appears expressly to
identify the two, viz. PA7. 22 Cc, but on
the principle that things which are equal
to the same thing are equal to one an-
other, the identification is complete, and
I have therefore thought myself at liberty
throughout the notes occasionally to illus-
trate Plato’s metaphysics by his theology.

phrase is no ground for interfering with

505 B—506 A What then ts the
Good? The majority answer ‘Pleasure,’
others, who are more refined, ‘Knowledge.’
Neither of these views ts tenable. Men
are constantly disputing about the Good,
but its existence ts practically admitted by
all, for tt is the ultimate object of all
endeavour. The Idea of the Good must
be known by our Guardians; for unless
they know the connexion between the Good,
and particular instances of the just, the
honourable etc., they cannot guard the
latter, or even indeed be said to hnow them
in any adequate measure.

I2 Tots pev toddols KTA. We need
not (with Tietzel Die Za. d. Guten u. d.
Gottesbegriff p. 9) find in this an allusion
to Aristippus and the Cyrenaics. Plato
means what he says and no more.
Pleasure is always the summum bonum
of the Many: cf. Arist. 2th. Nic. 1
3.109516. In rots kouworépors Diimmler
(Antisth. p. 43) and others have recog-
nised Antisthenes: cf. RP” § 218B x. b.
Hermann (Gesch. u. System p. 329 2. 323)
interprets the words—more correctly, I
think—of Socrates (cf. Xen. Mem. 1v 5.6)
and his immediate followers, Antisthenes
included. The Megarians sometimes
held the same view (D. L. 11 106). See
also next note.

portant possession for man: see on ef 6é
Mi iopev 5054. The present discussion
removes the Zezitéo princtpit by explaining
what the lé¢a rod d-yaod really means.

II. dyvnp kadh@s II: dvip kad@s Al: dvyp Kadds A

———— ee

usual distinction between Particulars and
Ideas, the former apprehended by Sight,
the latter by Reason. In the case of most
of the senses, nothing ts required except
the faculty and its object in order that a
sensation may take place. But in order
that we may see, a third requisite ts
necessary, viz. Light. Now the author of
Light is the Sun, and we may therefore
say that the Sun is the cause of Sight.
We must not identify either Sight or the
Eye with the Sun, although the Eye re-
sembles the Sun more closely than any
other organ of sense, and the Sun himself
ts seen by the Eye.

ledge or Pleasure is the Chief Good
(208 ff., 608). See also on 505c and
500 E.

xrh. The emphasis on 7d viv elvat
and 7a viv seems to hint that a de-

casion. But there is no dialogue in
which the Idea of Good is so clearly
described as in the Republic, and it is not
without reason that every historian of
Philosophy regards this passage as the /ocus
classieus on the subject. O. Schneider
( Versuch einer genet. Entw. d. Pl. aya bv

Philebus is unsuitable; and of the ®.A6-

coos we know nothing. Iam inclined to
think—in view especially of Bovdoluny av

adrois 6v ait&v—, he never, at all events

certain sense, perhaps, the 77z/aeus de-

the ‘Father of all’ not in himself so much

conclusion (l.c. p. 7§) that Plato could
hardly have depicted the Idea of Good at
all except by means of a comparison.
Certainly nothing else could have made

ds 8& exyovos KTA. The éxyovos

10 Kaladré 87 KT. The literal mean-

example. We postulate both ro\\a

believe that there is but one: see x

20 tori $ TUKTA. Steinhart (p. 689 2.
213) and others remark on Plato’s error
in denying that a medium is necessary in
Hearing etc. Aristotle was well aware of
this fact (see his de Av. 11 7. 419% 25 ff),
and there are several indications that it
was not altogether unknown to Plato. In
Tim. 67 8, for example, air is regarded as
in a certain sense the medium of sound.
Here, however, where a scientific analysis
of perception is not proposed, Plato takes
his stand upon the broad fact of experience,
that whereas we can hear, touch, etc.
either in light or in darkness, we can see
only where there is light.

508A 4 Tdv & odpavd Oedv: the
heavenly constellations, which form the
ovpdviov Oewy yévos (Tzm. 40 A). Plato’s
description of the sun is instinct with
religious feeling. ‘The ‘clear god and
patron of all light, From whom each
lamp and shining star doth borrow
The beauteous influence that makes him
bright’ claims adoration from Plato not
merely as an ovpdvios Oeds, like the
other stars, but as the symbol and scion
(€xyovos) of the Supreme Idea or God.
Cf. Bonitz Disp. Plat. duae, p. 6 n. 3, and
especially Paul Shorey in Chicago Studies
in Cl. Phil. Vol. I pp. 224 ff. The sun-
worship of some of the Neo-Platonists
was inspired in no small measure by this
passage of the Republec; see in particular
Julian’s ‘Address to the Sovereign Sun’
(els tov Bactéa ror) Or. Iv. Cf.
508 D 2.

508 B 9 *AvoaderrdtHv. The Eye
is the Body’s Sun: cf. Ar. Zhesm. 16, 17
@ pev Brérew xph pcr’ éunxavyicaro |
6pbarudv dvrinimoy mrALov Tpox@. A
similar idea appears in St Matth. 6. 226
Abxvos Tob cwuards éorw 6 dbhOadubs.
Conversely, the Sun is often in Greek
poetry called the Eye of the World or of
Day, and Shakespeare invokes the Sun
in the words ‘O eye of Eyes!’ (Rage of
Lucrece): cf. also Milton Par. Lost v 171
‘Thou Sun! of this great world both eye
and soul.’ Both comparisons rest ulti-
mately on the favourite Greek idea of the
Universe as the Macrocosm, and Man as
the Microcosm. See on this subject
Zeller? 11 2. p. 488, 111 2. pp. 136, 397 27,

and Stein Psych. d. Stoa l pp. 205—214.

II_ ovkoty «TA. Pindar 4%. 107
(Bergk) expresses the same idea in the
language of poetry: ’Axrls deXlov, tb 7o-
Nokon’ éujoao, Oowy warTep dumarov.

Tv Sivap.v is of course Thy ddvamuy
Tod opay, ‘the power of seeing.’ The
translation ‘faculty’ (D. and V.) for dvva-
uy is incorrect; for the faculty of dys is
supposed to be present in the eyes even
when there is no light (507 D). But the
eye has no ‘power’ to see, i.e. cannot
exercise the faculty of dyis unless such a
power is constantly dispensed (rapuevo-
Hévnv) to it from the Sun. Cf. Biehl dze
Ld. d. Guten p. 52, where the same view
is taken. The word émlppurov (‘flowing
over,’ ‘overflowing it,’ cf. Zim. 80 D) as
well as rauevouévyy unmistakably points
the allusion to Light. See also on érav
Hev krX. and robro rolyuy KTX. in 508 D.

508 B—509 a So much for our
similitude. The interpretation is as fol-
lows. The offspring and image of the
Good ts the Sun, whose relation to Sight
and its objects is the same as that of the
Good to Thought (vobs) and the objects of
Thought. The analogue of Light is
Truth ; as we cannot see without Light,
so, where Truth is absent, we cannot know.
The Idea of the Good is the source of Truth
and Knowledge, although itself apprehended
by Knowledge. As Light and Sight re-
semble the Sun, so Truth and Knowledge
resemble the Good, but the Good is not
zdentical with either, for it transcends
both.

29 Tovto Tolvuy KTA. The following
equations are involved :

témos Oparis =Tbros vonrés.

(1) Sun = Idea of Good.

(2) Light = Truth.

(3) We ty see of Know-
(Colours) ledge (Ideas).

(4) ae =|Knowing Subject.

(5) “Sei of _)Organ of noe

8 ledge (vofs).
(6) Faelty 6 "ot ae aculty of Reason
ight
(6 (v005).
ve)

(7) Exercise of ree of Reason
Sight (vods i.e. vdnots,
(dyes, Ae emo Th-

Tenn

(8) oe to = aby to know.

With hae to (2), Light has been
variously ead as symbolizing the
Idea of Good (Plotinus, as appears from
XXIII 4),
pp- 212 ff.), and the Ideas (Susemihl
Gen. Entw. 1 pp. 195 ff.). But the
chiasmus in 508 E, 509 A (émcorhunv—
éyw) clearly establishes equation (2) as
well as (6), and the entire simile is

Reason (Steinhart, A7nleitung

plunged in confusion if Light is equated
with anything except Truth. Cf. Stumpf
l.c. p. 60 zz. and Biehl l.c. pp. 50—53.

Plato means that as Light, coming from
the Sun, enables colours to be seen, and
the faculty of Sight to see, so Truth (or
rather Trueness, as Bosanquet remarks),
coming from the Good, enables the Ideas
to be known, and the faculty of vois to
know. It should be carefully noted that
Truth (or its source, the Idea of Good)
is not yet regarded as creating, but only
as actualizing the faculty of Reason.

The conception of the Good as the ulti-
mate cause of all Existence follows later
(509 B ff.): here it is represented only as
the cause of Knowledge. See also on
490 B, 508 D (dray pwev xrid.). If we
would grasp the full significance of
Plato’s comparison, we must not be con-
tent with the merely philosophical inter-
pretation of Light, but remember also
the many poetical and religious associa-
tions which attached themselves to such
words as @és and ¢éyyos, especially in
the Mysteries: see Neil on Ar. Knights
1319, Mommsen Feste d. Stadt vie pp.

229f., 238 f. and Hatch on The influence
of the Mysteries upon Christian usages
in his “zbbert Lectures pp. 283—3009.

The prominent position occupied by Light
in the half-religious, half-philosophical
teaching of Plotinus (see Zeller? 111 2 pp.

498 f., 500 2. 2, 616 al.) may to a large
extent be attributed to the elaboration and
expansion of the mystical elements in-
volved in Plato’s simile, the whole of
which, together with the similes of the
Line and the Cave, is of the greatest
importance for the history of Neopla-
tonism. Cf. also 508 A, B zn.

ywaoews (‘the exercise of knowledge,” cf.
épacts, vénovs and the like) below. Plato’s

509Aa—c Jn the second place the Sun

5098 ff. 8 Tov HAvov KTA. The
Good has been shewn to be the cause of
Knowledge: Socrates now proceeds to
shew that it is also the cause of Being.
In the philosophy of Plato, Knowledge
is the epistemological counterpart of
Being, Being the ontological counter-
part of Knowledge: see V 470 E ff. 27.
The final unity in which both Knowledge
and Being meet is the Idea of the Good,

which is therefore the supreme and
ultimate cause of the Universe. See
also on ovK ovolas xrA. below and the

Appendix to Book vil On Plato’s Dialectic.
