13 ovK ovolas KTA. has occasioned a
vast amount of discussion. Krohn boldly
declares that ‘Die l6éa rod dyaGod ist
keine Idee, denn sie hat keine ovcla,
sondern es ist eine Macht, die mit un-
serer Denkweise nur als die Gottheit
begriffen werden kann’ (P/. St. p. 146).
Fouillée La Philosophie de Platon 11 p.
10g draws an over-subtle distinction be-
tween elyac and ovola, holding that
although the Good is not ovcla, it never-
theless is dv (cf. vil 518 c). Others have
suspected the text, O. Schneider, for
example, proposing ob <ydvov> ovctas
KTr. (Versuch einer genet. Entw. d. Plat.
dyaév p. 16). That the text is right,
the balance with od yéveow—évra con-
clusively shews. The Sun, said Socrates,
is the cause of yéveous, though not himself
yéveots. Just so the Good is the cause of
ovola, though not itself ovcla, but (to use
a Neoplatonic expression) vsmepovctos.
Plato’s meaning is as follows. The Sun
is not ‘yéveots in the sense in which the
objects which he produces are yryvéueva.
Yet in a certain sense he too is yéveots,
for he is éparés: see VII 529 C ff. and
Tim. 28 B. (Bosanquet cannot be right in
denying that Plato regards the Sun as a
yyouevov). As the cause of yéveous, we
may, in fact, regard the Sun as the only
true yéveous, for all yvyvdueva are derived
from him. Similarly the Good is not
ovata in the sense in which the Ideas
are oclac; but in a higher sense it is
the only true ovoia, for all otcia: are only
specific determinations of the Good. The
barepovotdrns of the Good is merely
Plato’s way of saying that the first Prin-

ciple of all existence must itself be un-
derived. See on dpxnv dvurbderov 510B
and cf. Biehl l.c. p. 62 and Fouili¢e lc.
II pp. 105—r11, where the matter is very
clearly explained. The doctrine of the
birepovotdrns of the Highest afterwards
became a cardinal point with the Neo-
platonists : see Plotinus ap. RP.” p. 528,
and for other references Hermann Vind.
disp. de id. boni pp. 40 2. 84, 41 2. 87,
Zeller’ 111 2. pp. 490 ff., Fouillée Za Philo-
sophie de Platon 111 pp. 289, 291 7., and
Shorey Chicago Studies in Cl. Phil. 1p.
188 z. 1. It is highly characteristic of
Plato’s whole attitude that he finds the
true keystone of the Universe—the ulti-
mate fountain from which both Know-
ledge and Existence flow—in no cold
and colourless ontological abstraction,
like Being, but in that for which waéoa
n Ktlows cvorevager kal cvvwilver (Lom.
8. 22)—viz. 7d dyabbv. Cf. Phaed. 97
c ff. and see also on 508 D. The concep-
tion is poetical and religious no less than
philosophical, and may be compared with
Dante’s ‘L’ Amor che muove il Sole e I’
altre Stelle’ and Tennyson’s ‘ For so the
whole round earth is every way Bound
by gold chains about the feet of God,’ as
well as with Aristotle’s mp&tov xkwodv
dklyynrov Met. A 4 et al.

24. odpavod Rg: ovpavdv A. 27. 7d &g: om. A.

eee

request, now proceeds to expound the
similitude more fully, Let us take a
line, and divide it into two unequal
parts, to represent the objects of Sight
and the objects of Thought respectively.
Tf we further subdivide each part in the
ratio of the original sections, we shall
have four segments, representing, n order
of clearness, (1) Images and the like,
(2) so-called real things, (3) the objects
of that intellectual method which descends
Jrom assumptions to a conclusion, using
sensible objects as images or tllustrations,
(4) the objects of that intellectual method,
which ascends from assumptions to an
unassumed first principle, without making
use of any sensible illustrations whatso-
ever, and thereafter descends to a conclu-
sion. The third section represents the
subjects investigated by the so-called ‘ Arts’
or mathematical sciences ; the fourth ts the
sphere of Dialectic. The corresponding
mental states are called by Socrates elxacta,
mors, didvora, and vonots. Lach of these
is clear or sure exactly in proportion as tts
objects are true.

509 Dff. The simile of the Line
contains perhaps more Platonic teaching
than any passage of equal length in
Plato’s writings, and is of primary and
fundamental importance for the inter-
pretation of his philosophy. I have
discussed thervarious difficulties as they
occur, partly in the notes and partly in
the Appendices to this Book. For a
consecutive exposition of the whole
simile in its connexion with the simile
of the Cave see App. I to Book VII.

24 ‘va pr ovpavod KrA. ‘I donot say

‘of heaven,’ lest you should imagine that
I am etymologising on the name.” The
Sun might well be called Bacueds otpavod.
Socrates pretends to avoid the word
ovpavés, lest by thus equating it with
éparév (for the contrast with voyrod
would suggest that odpavod=dparod) he
should be accused of deriving ovpavds
from 6pay, as certain clever people did
in Plato’s time (Cvaz. 3968. The same
derivation is given by Philo Jud. de
mund. opif. 10). For ooplgecPa in this
sense cf. gopla in Crat. 396C,D. E.S.
Thompson (Proceedings of the Camb.
Phil. Soc. 1888 p. 14) takes coplfecPar
simply as ‘pun’ and thinks that the pun
is between vod (suggested in vonTod above)
and ovpa-vod, quoting dm’ dvov mecelv, and
the anecdote in D. L. 1 118, VI 3: cf.
also Isocrates Apophth. Fr. 8 ed. Blass.
But such a pun is both far-fetched and
pointless, and in view of the passage
from the Cratylus there should be no
doubt that Plato more suo is merely
scofing at a well-known contemporary
etymology. The reading ovpavdy—see
cr. m.—would be fatal to Thompson’s
theory, but o¥pavod (which most Mss read)
is more pointed and idiomatic, and per-
haps right, though the accusative is not
indefensible.

preferring tea, others, such as Richter

ica (which is certainly not Greek, though
found in a few inferior Mss); but there
should be no question that Plato wrote
dvioca. If the line is bisected, all four
segments are equal, and the elaborate
proportions drawn in §10 A, 511 E, VII
534 A represent no corresponding relations
between the different segments of the line.
The inequality, as Schneider and Stein-
hart point out, is intended to represent
the difference in cagpjvera or adjbem
between the Sofacrdv (or dparév) and the
ywucrdv (or vonrov): cf. capyveia cal
doapela below and 510 A. (So also
Benson in Nettleship’s Lect. and Rem.
II p. 2397.). For this reason the vonrév
should be represented by a longer seg-
ment. Others assign the larger part to
the dparév, as being the region of 7a
mo\dd (Plutarch lc. and Espinas in his
edition of Book v1), but the length of
the two main segments should follow the
primary and fundamental principle of
Plato’s classification. ‘The relevant con-
sideration is not at present multiplicity
versus unity, but different degrees of
clearness and truth. Beckman’s excision
of duca tunwara (num Plato artefactor-
um ideas statuerit p. 38) needs no refuta-
tion. See also next note.

10 é@€ tro0écewy. Jrd0eots is correctly
defined in the Platonic Spo: (415 B) as 4px)
dvarédeuxros, a starting-point which is
not demonstrated, but taken for granted,
assumed, postulated. The arithmetician,
for example, dorl@erat the odd, the even,
etc., ie. assumes that his definition of
odd, even, etc. is correct, and draws
conclusions from his §7é@eors of the odd,
the even, etc. by means of exclusively
deductive reasoning: cf. H. Sidgwick in
y. Ph. i p. too. If we attack his
jw60eo.s, as Lucian for example does
(Hermot. 74, quoted by Stallbaum), he
must, gwé arithmetician, throw up the
sponge, for the droGéces of the inferior
vonrév can be demonstrated (or over-
thrown) only by Dialectic. Cf. generally
Men. 86 © ff. Schneider may be right
in supposing that Aristotle had the
present passage in view when he wrote

ted. Adyw, once proposed by Hermann,
has nothing in its favour. {nre? must be
supplied to govern 70 @repov.

adpxiv avumdlerov. The only dpxi
dyumoeros is the Idea of the Good:
cf. vil 532Af. Towards this the Dia-

lectician travels, starting from d7o6é-.

ges. He may begin, for example, by
‘assuming’ the ‘just.? In such a case
he assumes that his definition of ‘just’
is correct, i.e. corresponds exactly to the
Idea of ‘Just.’ But whereas the arith-
metician treats his dé0eous as an ultimate
truth, and proceeds deductively to a
conclusion, making use of sensible images
by way of illustration, the dialectician
treats his hypothesis as purely pro-
visional, testing, revising, rejecting (VII
533 C #.), and reconstructing, and
gradually ascending step by step to the
first principle of all (riv rod wavros dp-
xiv), without employing any sensible
objects to illustrate his reasoning. The
one gives no account of his brédeots (ov-
déva Aoyov—gavepSv in C below); the
other not only does, but must do so, just
because he zs a dialectician: cf. v11533Cff.
He connects his bof éce:s with others, sub-
suming them under higher and yet higher
—better and truer—vo@écers, until at
last he has traversed the whole region
of vontd. Such of his b7oécers as sur-
vive will be improved at each stage in
the ascent, and finally, as soon as the
Idea of Good is reached, all his surviving
dmrobéces will actually have become per-
fect counterparts of the Ideas which they
have hitherto been only assumed to re-
present. In the meantime the apy} Tod
mavrés, which Plato himself described
dogmatically 6:’ efxévos in 507 A—509 C,
will have ceased to be a mere v7é-
Oeots: it will have become, in the fullest
sense of the term, an dpx?) avumd0erTos:
for the highest rung of the ladder is not
reached until the entire domain of the
knowable has been exhausted, and shewn
to be the expression of the Idea of Good.
Plato’s ideal—it is no more—is a com-
prehensive and purely intellectual view
oi the totality of vonrd, in which every
department is seen in its connexion with
every other, and all in their dependence
on the Good, which is in itself dvumd-

Oeros and birepovcvos—dvurrdberos because
higher than all brodéces and itself
proved by an exhaustive scrutiny of all
vontd, wmepovo.os because higher than,
and the cause of, all existence. See also
on 511 B and the Appendix to Book vil
On Plato’s Dialectic, together with Jack-
son J. of Ph. X pp. 145 f., where the
distinctive peculiarities of the two methods
are very clearly explained.

12 dvmep exetvo etkdvav: ig. dvev
trav alomep éxeivo (mre?) elkdvwv. I
formerly read ré&v sept éxelvo elxdvav
(with g), but now think (with Schneider
and others) that A is right. The attrac-
tion of a relative in the dative case is
rare, but not unexampled. Van Cleef
(de attract. in enunt. rel. usu Plat. p.
45) cites Gorg. 509 A, Prot. 361 E, Theact.
144A, Rep. VII 531 E (all examples of
évtuyxdvw, whose proper construction in
the sense of ‘fall in with’ is the dative,
not the genitive), and Zp. VII 327A
(with mpocéruxov) ; for examples in other
authors see Kiihner Gv. Gv. II p. 914.
If dvev and éxeivo are pronounced with
emphasis, the meaning, I think, is easily
caught. Stallbaum reads wv epi «rh.

autos 6 Adyos KTA.: ‘the argument

10 Svvdper should not be translated
‘faculty,’ but simply ‘power’ (cf. 508 E7z.):
the argument, unaided by elxdves (avrds
‘by itself,’ cf. avrots eldeot 510 B 2.), grasps
its object by the inherent power of dia-
lectical argumentation (d.aAéyeo Aac), and
nothing else. In spite of Grimmelt (de
réip. unit. etc. p. 52) it is certainly an
error to identify 6 Aédyos with vois. Why
does Dialectic dispense with all sensible
images or illustrations? Plato (it should
be remembered) holds that the intrusion
of any element of sense-perception, how-
ever small, impedes the exercise of
thought: see Phacd. 79 c ff. The t7o-

Gécets of the dialectician may be and
often are generalisations from aic@nrd,
but a generalisation, regarded in itself, is
wholly vonrév. These brobécets it is the
province of Dialectic to test in every
possible way, to demolish where necessary
(VII 533 C 2.), to correct by one another,
to classify according to their mutual
coherence and interdependence, until by
an exhaustive scrutiny of all vontd we
grasp the unifying principle of all exist-
ence—the Idea of the Good. Cf. vii
517 Cz. and see on 700 dvumobérov below
and the Appendix to Book v1 On Plato’s
Dialectic.

II tod dvumobérov. See on doxtpy
avuTd0erov 510 B. Plato makes no at-
tempt in the Republic to classify Ideas
in such an ascending scale as he here
suggests, though it is probable from
509 A that Knowledge and Truth would
rank near to the Good. Nor is there any
dialogue in which an exhaustive classifi-
cation is even attempted. Such hints as
Plato gives us throughout his writings are
enumerated in Stumpf das Verhdliniss etc.
pp- 50, 50, 76, and in Zeller* 11 1, pp.
704—707: cf. also Fouillée La Philoso-
phie de Platon 11 pp. 99—104- We must
suppose that each higher Idea will excel
all the lower both in range and in excel-
lence. These two characteristics are, from
Plato’s point of view, the same. The
wider an Idea is in range and extension,
the greater will be the sum of existences
of which it is the cause. But the Idea
of Good is the cause of all existence, so
that each higher Idea will be better than
all below it, because it contains more of |

Good. Beyond this it is perhaps safer

12 wdAwat «tA. The dialectician’s
progress involves both an ascent and a
descent—an ascent éml rhv dpxjv, and
a descent dd rijs doxfs él Thy redeuTHy
(cf. Aristotle quoted on 510 B). By the
time that he reaches the Idea of the
Good, all his surviving Jo@éoes have
become exact counterparts of the Ideas
which are their objective correlates; the
others have all of them been demolished
(vil §33C#.). The conclusions (reAevrat)
of dialectic are therefore impregnable;
wevdns emioriun is a contradiction in
terms (Vv 477 £%.). For more on this
subject see the Appendix to Book vi1 On
Plato’s Dialectic.

24 as—otoav. didvom is the most
general word for a state (8s) of mind or
mode of thought in Greek; and the
limitation here introduced is entirely
Plato’s own. Plato apparently attempts
to fortify his innovation by etymology,

26 véonorv is used in its strict sense of

5115 27 awlotwektr. If we strictly
limit DC to épard, micris must be under-
stood as the state of mind which believes
only in visible, palpable (évapyq) things
(ra wepl juds Spa Kal way 7d puTevTdy Kal
7d oxevacrov doy yévos 510A): ‘seeing,’
as we still say, ‘is believing.’ But Plato
has already spoken of AC as dogacrdéy
(510A 2); so that mlors should not be
confined to the objects of sight. It is in
fact a subdivision of dd6éa, superior in
point of ‘clearness’ (cagjveta) to elkacla.
We may regard it as the normal con-
dition of the average uneducated mind.
elxacia is the state of mind in which
elxdves are held to be true. Here again,
if eixdves are strictly limited to images of
dpard (cf. 509 E, 510 A), elxacla must be
similarly confined in its scope, and loses
all metaphysical interest and importance:
see VIL517A72. But since the efxdves are
a lower grade of dofacrd (510 A %.),
elxagla should be understood as a lower
variety of d6£a (as in VII 534 A), viz. the
state of mind which accepts as true that
which is a copy of a copy (tplrov mpods
adnGeav). In this sense elkacla (with
a play on elxdéves) is a new coinage of
Plato’s. The translation ‘conjecture’ is
misleading, for conjecture implies con-
scious doubt or hesitation, and doubt is
foreign to elxacla in Plato’s sense. Plato
may however have intended to suggest
that such a state of mind is in reality no

Schneider’s translation of this sentence is as follows :—“ wie aber zu
steuern sei, es mégen nun einige wollen oder nicht, davon glauben sie
nicht dass es eine Kunst und Uebung gebe, mit der man dann eben die
Steuermannskunst habe.” The view which I have given in the notes is
in general harmony with this interpretation ; but I think that the word
wedérn denotes ‘study’ rather than actual ‘practice’ (“ Uebung” or
“ exercitatio” Schneider): see the notes.

The strength of Schneider’s explanation lies in its conformity with
the whole course of Plato’s argument both here and in the passages
which I have cited from the Politicus. In particular, the exact parallel
between édv ré tives Bovhovras edv Te py and éav re Exdvras éav Te GKkovTas
(in Pol. 293 B) appears to me the strongest possible confirmation of the
general soundness of his view. No interpretation that I know of,
Schneider’s alone excepted, assigns its proper force to éav ré twes
BobrA\wvras (cf. édv re Exdvras, Pol. |.c.) as well as to éav te py. The
true pilot cares just as little whether people wish him to steer as whether
they do not: his art has nothing whatever to do with the sentiments
with which his passengers regard his rule. Schneider’s interpretation is
also supported by the emphatic pyre réxvqv—prjte pederyv: the false
pilot will not allow that you can learn ‘either art or theory’ of how to
steer, because according to him there is absolutely nothing technical or
theoretical about steering. The only ‘art of steering’? which he will
admit is the art of collaborating with himself in order to get command
of the ship (vavrixév pév Kadodvtas Kal KuBepynteKov Kal érirrapevov To
Kata vatv os av EvdAapBavew dewds 7 dmws apgovow xrAr.). It must,
however, be admitted that dua kal ryv kvBepvyrixyy is not altogether
easy on Schneider’s view. The reader naturally expects tiv KuBepvytixiv
to be different from the réyvn and pedéry: for on a first examination of
the passage, Plato seems to be speaking of the impossibility of acquiring
at the same time two different arts: whereas Schneider holds that the two
arts are the same, ryv kuBepvytixyy being only Plato’s way of expressing
the réyvn and pedérn tovrov dus KvBepvycet.

Is it possible to devise any explanation which, while agreeing in the
main with Schneider’s, will escape the apparent difficulty to which I
have just drawn attention?

We note that «uBepyytumy has already been implicitly defined by the
sailors as the art of helping them to get command (xvBepyytixdy xrh.

APPENDICES TO BOOK V1. 75.

above). Why then should we not suppose that the sailors ‘think it
impossible to acquire an art of how to steer (Srws kvBepyyjoet) along with
KuBepvytixy’ in their sense of the term, i.e. (practically) along with the
art of making themselves masters of the ship? ‘This interpretation, as
far as concerns the language, seems to me possible enough; but it
implies that the sailors do think it possible to learn the art of how to
steer independently and by itself: whereas they have already said that
such an art cannot be taught at all (facxovras yndé SiSaxrdv eivar 488 B).
For this among other reasons I think that this solution should not be
entertained.

I have endeavoured in the notes to justify Schneider’s explanation
of dua cai, and here it need only be added that one source of embar-
rassment is the tendency which we feel to give to dua more of a strictly
temporal signification than necessarily belongs to it in that idiomatic
phrase.

An entirely different view of the sentence is taken by Ast and
others. According to Ast, Plato is here distinguishing between two arts,
viz. (1) scientific pilotage (the knowledge of astronomy etc.), and
(2) the “ars imperandi—ut quae scientia et ars ipsum doceant, a multitu-
dine ipsi subdita fieri curet.” yy kuBepyvntuxnv is (1), and réxvnv
rovrov (2). On this view Plato asserts that the crew in general consider
it impossible to acquire both the art of steering (ryv kuBepyytixyy) and
that of steering whether people wish it or no (drws kuBepyice Téxvnv
rovrov), i.e. in other words, enforcing and maintaining authority. Both
arts—so Ast interprets—are united, according to Plato, in the true pilot.

This explanation Stallbaum apparently accepts, conjecturing only 77
KuBepvytixy for tiv KvBepvytucpv. Ido not deny that it can be elicited
from Plato’s language, if we take this sentence by itself: but it is open
to serious objection on the score of meaning, as has been pointed out
by H. Sidgwick (7. Px. v pp. 274—276), Richards (CZ. Kev. vimI p. 23),
and Shorey (4. 7. PA. xvi p. 234). It represents the sailors as
admitting that there is a ¢vwe art of steering, which under certain
circumstances it is possible to acquire. But this is precisely what they
deny (see 488 B pds € Tovros pdoxovtas pide didaxrdv elvor), nor
could they possibly admit it, so long as they ‘have not so much
as a notion that the true Pilot should study the year and the seasons’
etc. (488D). Their solitary notion of an art of steering is how to
get the helm into their hands (488¢ mdvra rowivtas dorws av odict
76 mydddvov émirpeyy and C, D vavtixdv pev KaAodvtas Kat KvB EpvnTLKoV
—és dv évd\AapBdvew Sewvos H xtdr.). Moreover, even if they were to
allow that there is an art of steering, they would certainly not allow
that others possessed it rather than themselves: cf, Pol. 302 a, B. Nor,
again, does Plato ever admit that the art of enforcing one’s authority
has anything whatever to do with the art oi steering. In the Podticus,
he is careful to point out that they are entirely distinct: see the passages
referred to in the note.

1 For another special discussion of the passage see Richter in Fleckeisen’s

Jowett and Campbell’s explanation is somewhat different. They
do not suppose that Plato himself means to attribute to the true pilot
both knowledge and power to enforce his authority ; but apparently

kuBepvytixy, which can be acquired by itself, but cannot be combined
with the art of getting possession of the helm. ‘This view 1s open

Sidgwick and Richards both agree with Grote (//ato II p. 80) that

enforce one’s authority as well as knowledge of steering. According to

olouéve, in agreement with ord, this particular difficulty disappears. I
formerly accepted Sidgwick’s proposal, but there is force in Richards’
criticism that “the sentence would be most clumsy in form, nor is it to
the point what the true steersman thinks; Plato is describing the state
of mind of the crew.” 8é after dzrws is also a difficulty.

On the whole, I am now inclined to think that Schneider’s interpre-
tation has the most numerous and important arguments in its favour,
and for this reason I have adopted it in the notes.

that it is the Aveopagiticus, and not the Panegyricus, of which Plato

that dvdpecxeAov meant anything beyond “imago hominis,” but he retracts
this view in his Addctamenta (p. 49) and Translation.

Schneider’s text is followed also by Baiter. The majority of editors
prefer kal mpds éxeivo at 76 év tots dvOpdrous eprrovoiev Evppcyvivres KrA.

His constitution is not a compromise between for example Athens and

of earthly existence will allow—in other words such a polity as is
described in the Republic. The Philosopher-king is not the man to
paint an imperfect picture, though he knows that it will be but a picture
after all,

Such is the reading of A and II: and none of the other mss have

Of the qualities named, the following appear in the description
of the philosophic character (485 C—487 A): cvpddea, wvypy, peyar
Aorpérea. It is also clear that ofor xoopéws—lyv represents owppoowy
(485 £). dyxivor and ééeis certainly refer to intellectual qualities, as
appears from their combination with edpabeis Kat wvypoves, and with ooa
@dra rtovrow ererar: cf. also Zheaet. 144A. veavixot ‘spirited’ has af-

80 APPENDICES TO BOOK VI.

finities with dv8petor (486 B—487 a), but Vermehren (P/. Stud. pp. 983—
103) is mistaken in holding that the two notions are identical.

According to Schneider, who retains the reading of the Mss,
cipabeis—peyadompereis tas Siavoias forms the predicate, the subject
being ofo.—{jv. This interpretation gives the right sense, but is other-
wise forced and unnatural in the last degree. Moreover, if ofo is the
subject, we can scarcely dissociate oi towotro from it, and even on
Schneider’s view of rovodrou refers to evpabeis—diavoias.

Stallbaum prints a comma after dveoGou, and understands Plato to
mean that a combination of the intellectual virtues enumerated in
ciuabeis—édéeis is itself rare, as well as the union of spirit and sobriety.
Such a view, even if grammatically possible, is certainly awkward; and
we have every reason to suppose that Plato did not consider the union
of kindred intellectual qualities as in any way exceptional. It is the
union of certain intellectual and moral virtues with certain other moral
virtues which he considers rare: cf. Zheaet. 144 A, B, the whole of which
passage is important for the understanding of Plato here.

Others, such as Vermehren l.c. and J. and C., suppose that ofo.—fjv
is a consequence of veavixoi—peyadompereis. This is however (as Shorey
points out A. 7. Pk. xv1 p. 236) opposed to every statement made by
Plato on the subject. The opposition between Ouvpoeidés, veavixor,
év8petov (by which is meant, in this particular contrast, the active side
of Courage) and zpaov, kdop.ov, oddpor etc., is regarded by him as the
fundamental antithesis of human character: see for example 11 375 C,
Ill 399 C, 410D, Pol. 306 Cff., 307 C, 309 E, 311 B, Zim. 18 a and Laws
731B. We have no right to hide this fact by explaining away veavixdr.
The word means ‘full of youthful vigour,’ ‘spirited,’ and nothing more.
veavixév is of course a constituent factor in dvdpelov, but it is not identi-
cal with it, nor do any of the passages which Vermehren cites (Zheaet.
168.c, Rep. 425 C, 491 E, 563 £, Lys. 204 E, Alc. 104 A) prove any such
identity. Still less does the word mean “generous” (J. and C.), or
“kraftig, mannhaft, und s¢ttlich tichtig” (Vermehren).

We may therefore be certain that veavixoi—peyaAompemets is opposed
to ofo.—ffv. The ms reading will admit of this only if we construe
¢vecOar with ofc, and suppose that kat veavxoi—éduavoias is added
parenthetically as a sort of afterthought. This was Schleiermacher’s
view, and it is also one of J. and C.’s alternatives. It is certainly right
in sense, but the construction is intolerably ambiguous and awkward
(cf. Susemihl, Gen. Lntw. 11 p. 191 2.).

Various solutions are possible. I formerly (with Heindorf on
Theaet. 144A) transposed xai, reading pvecOar veavixoi—xat otor xrA.
Heindorf’s remedy is however not quite satisfactory, because it lays all
the stress upon the difficulty of finding the two opposite kinds of mora?
qualities united with intellectual sagacity, whereas—as appears from the
next sentence—the relevant point is that intellectual vivacity and
acumen are seldom found along with ove of the two phases of moral
character, viz. sobriety and stedfastness. For this reason I now venture
on the transposition printed in the text. It is worthy of note that in
two places where the scribe of A omitted a passage of some length,

The difficulties of this sentence have hardly received sufficient
attention at the hands of editors.

If xar iSéayv—the reading of all the mss—is genuine, what is the
grammatical object of rievres ?

Two possibilities suggest themselves. One is to understand év or
the like, as parallel to air xaddv and ar'ré ayabdv. This view is
apparently adopted by Schneider, who translates “Und dann ein schones
selbst und ein gutes selbst und so bei allem, was wir dort als vieles
setzten, wiederum eis nach der angenommenen Einheit des Begriffs
eines jeden setzend nennen wir jedes das, was ist.” Stallbaum seems
to have taken a similar view, although his note is not quite explicit.
But it is so difficult to supply év as an object to riHévres that we must,
I think, reject this interpretation altogether. The second and more
plausible alternative is—with Prantl, and the English editors and trans-
lators—to regard the object of riévres as identical with that of ériGepev.
But kar’ iSéav play rievres is far from clear. tOévres ought clearly to
be understood in the same sense as érifewev, and the whole phrase
should express the antithesis of ws woAAd érifewev. We are hardly
justified in translating ruBévax kar’ id€ay piay as ‘reduce to a single form’
(D. and V.) or ‘bring under a single idea’ (Jowett), although the phrase
might possibly here mean ‘regard as belonging to one Idea.’ If ka7’
iSéav is sound, the least unsatisfactory course is perhaps to print a colon
after éré@ewev, and explain thus: ‘reversing our procedure, we view
them as falling under a single Idea of each,...and call each that-which-
is’: i.e. for example dixarov, eadpor etc., each of which we took as moAXd,
we now regard as belonging to or falling under one Idea of dékatov, one
of aG¢pov etc. But neither this interpretation nor any other which
I can devise (such as ‘regard according to’ or ‘in the light of’ one Idea
of each) furnishes a thoroughly clear and simple sense, or a satisfactory
antithesis to ws woAAd éribewev. The only suitable contrast is that
between the one Idea itself and the woAde. For this reason I have ven-
tured to replace xar by xaé. The occurrence of ras 8’ ad idéas in the next
sentence is also, so far as it goes, in favour of the proposed correction.

If we read xaé for xard, we ought certainly (with Bosanquet) to take
8 %orw exaorov as a secondary predicate after rpocayopevouev. The
translation “we call each ‘that-which-is’” (Schneider and others) is
grammatically possible and even necessary, I think, if xara is retained;
but it will scarcely be denied that 6 éorw Exaorov, taken by itself, is

Schneider boldly understands adrois as tots dupacw, referring to Zim,
67cff., where it is virtually said that Colour, in an act of Sight, éuarimrec
cis rhv Sv. But a careful study of the analysis of Sight in the Zimaeus
will shew that Schneider’s view is not supported by that dialogue. If
év adrois = év Tois éupacu, it is clear from the rest of the sentence that
Plato thinks Colour may be present in the eyes even where there is
no light. But in the Zimaeus Colour does not éumiarev eis THV OW
when light is absent: see 45C with Archer-Hind ad loc. The fact is
that both in the Zimaeus and in the Republic Colour is regarded as
inherent in things and not in the eyes: see Zim. 67 C xpdas éxadeoaper,
rdya tSv cwpdtov Exdorwv droppéoveay and Rep. 508C Gv ay Tas xpoas

By Schmelzer and the Oxford editors, adrots is interpreted as rois
Sparois “from rod oparod supra.” Plato certainly allows himself great
latitude in matters of this kind, but not, I think, where the result would
be that he must inevitably be misunderstood ; and even an intelligent
reader might here be forgiven if he referred odrois to tots oppacw.

Various corrections have been proposed. év av tots <oparots> is
Richards’ conjecture; but Plato is careful throughout this whole dis-
cussion to use éparév only in the singular, as a general term for the
visible: cf. 508c, 509D (bis). For ‘things seen’ he uses opwpeva 507C,
508A, 508C, 510D. On this account év ad tots <épwpévors> or (better)
év adrots <Tols dpwpévous> appears to me preferable, if the passage is to
be emended on these lines.

In CZ. Rev. x11 p. 99 I ventured to submit another emendation.
It will be observed that in the latter part of the sentence Plato treats
the colours themselves as the objects of vision (ta te xpwpara <orae
ddépata), without alluding to the visible objects in which the colours
inhere. Cf. also Zim. 67C¢ ff., and Arist. de An, 7. 418 26 ff.
Hence I proposed to read wapovons d& xpdas, ev adrois eo py Tapa-
yéerntat KrX., ice. ‘unless they’ (viz. 7 dys and xpda Ss. 7a xpwpara) ‘are re-

So A reads. The only important variant is 64 vod (Hv and several
other Mss) for d:avood.

Jowett and Campbell, following Schneider and Hermann, retain the
text of A, and translate as follows: ‘‘This then, which imparts truth to
the things that are known and gives to the knower the power of
knowing, is what I would have you call the idea of good: and this
you will deem to be the cause of knowledge and of truth so far as the
latter is known: but fair as are both these, knowledge and truth, you
will be right in thinking that it is something fairer than these.” pv
is explained as belonging strictly to airfav and “opposed to the following
8é: the idea of good is indeed (ev) the cause of knowledge and truth,
but (8é) it is other and fairer than they.” Schneider’s explanation and
translation differs hardly at all from that of the Oxford editors.

The above rendering is open to grave objections both on the score
of grammar and of sense. Siavocicfa. can hardly be used with a
participle (otcav) without ws: and os yryvwoxopevys surely cannot mean
‘so far as known.’ It is also, to say the least, extremely difficult to
explain the position of wév. 5d. tov éyyeyovdra pev épwra, which Schneider
(Addit. p. 51) cites from X 6078, is a very remote parallel. Nor is
there any point, so far as I can discover, in saying that the Idea of the
Good is the cause of truth so far as truth is known. The Idea
of the Good is the cause of all Truth, known and unknown. And
